==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This master’s thesis explores the intersection of design and systems science, with the
primary objective of formulating a process model to address intricate systemic challenges
and guide towards equitable transformation within the system. The research method­
ology encompassed a systematic literature review of existing systemic design process
models in academic literature and action research, including interviews with experts and
a workshop to validate the derived process model’s applicability in promoting sustainable
transformation in the agri­food sector.
The process model serves as a guide for actors, aiding them in unfolding existing struc­
tures and systemic powers that contribute to the current problematic situation. It further
assists in exploring potential future scenarios and actionable pathways toward the devel­
opment of an ideal situation. Actors are encouraged to continuously reflect on the sys­
tem’s boundaries and the interventions they have introduced in the system. The model
extends the mental models of the actors, fosters learning about the system, and provides
a systemic approach for transformative action in the face of complex systemic challenges.

==PAGE:6==
Introduction
1.1
Background on systems
A natural starting point for this report is to delve into the concept of a system. The foun­
dation for this explanation is derived from the opening chapter of D. Meadows’ book,
”Thinking in Systems: A Primer” [1]. In her perspective, a system is defined as ”an inter­
connected set of elements that is coherently organized in a way that achieves something”.
Elements, interconnections and function or purpose are essential components of the sys­
tem. However, it’s crucial not to view the system merely as an aggregation of these
parts; systems are characterized by integrity, wholeness, and a specific behaviour aimed
at maintaining that integrity. Meadows states that posing the question of which elements
are most important is inherently an unsystematic question, as every element is significant.
Nevertheless, altering one or another would yield a significantly different impact within the
system [1].
Systems, however, can be seen as endless, due to all the elements being connected in
one way or another. The concept of boundaries becomes crucial to allow one to decide
what is included and excluded in the system that one is analysing [2]. In a way, setting
these boundaries in the system can be perceived as defining the system.
1.1.1
Systemic intervention/ transformation
Systemic Intervention
G. Midgley’s Systemic Intervention methodology is chosen in this research as a foundation
for analyzing to what extent the systems thinking approach is reflected within the inves­
tigated systemic design process models. In the upcoming few paragraphs, the Systemic
Intervention methodology will be defined in more detail and concepts from the method­
ology will be discussed to lay down the aspects to be considered in the process model
analysis.
The upcoming few paragraphs are based on the volume “Systemic Intervention: Philos­
ophy, Methodology, and Practice” by Gerald Midgley, from the “Contemporary Systems
Thinking” series, editor Robert L. Flood [2].
Intervention, Agent and Boundary Judgement.
1
==PAGE:7==
Systemic intervention is the methodology proposed by G. Midgley that merges systems’
ideas and the concept of intervention. Intervention is described as a “purposeful action by
a human agent to create change.” Before introducing the concept of intervention further, it
is important to mention that Midgley uses the term ‘agent’ to refer to an individual human
being or an interacting group (e.g., family, community) that has assigned purposes. The
group does not necessarily share the “purpose of the whole”, but can still be identified
as an agent if it can be perceived as moving towards one direction to realize a group’s
“dominant purpose”, despite the possible opposing views. The actions of the agent are
founded on the knowledge available to the agent (including perceptions, implicit under­
standings, unconscious motivations, behaviour habits, etc.). Hence, “action can be said
to be undertaken by an agent under the influence of the knowledge­generating system
in which s/he is embedded”. This knowledge­generating system can be defined through
boundary judgment. Boundary judgment is described later in this chapter.
Intervention and Observation
In his writing, G. Midgley presents the concept of intervention mostly by contrasting it
with the concept of observation. He cites other authors who state that an approach can
only be considered scientific if high­quality and independent observation is achieved [3],
meaning that the object of investigation is in no way impacted by the observer. Their de­
rived conclusion is that scientific observation can be controlled by eliminating the agent’s
intervention. However, G. Midgley suggests two arguments as to why independent ob­
servation is not attainable. Firstly, in the system’s view, everything in the universe is
interconnected, so what is observed cannot be independent of the observer. Secondly,
knowledge requires a knowledge­generating system giving rise to it, meaning every obser­
vation is influenced by the presence of the observer. To give an example, moral choices
will always be made regarding the object of investigation or the use of the study findings.
Additionally, observation involves interpretation ”mediated by conceptual and emotional
frameworks” and is expressed through the use of language (the topic of language is ex­
tensively discussed throughout the book. However, the crucial argument here is that it is
impossible for every word to have the same meaning to every person). G. Midgley not
only denies the possibility of independent observation but also argues that observation is
a form of intervention, an action purposefully undertaken by the agent to create change.
Boundary Critique
Returning to the fundamental idea that everything in the Universe is interconnected, the
decision on where to draw boundaries in the analysis becomes crucial. According to Midg­
ley G., when two groups engage in the process, the boundaries they set inevitably differ.
This difference creates a zone of marginalized elements, those excluded by the group with
a narrower boundary. Consequently, some stakeholders and issues may be stigmatized
within the systemic processes. The core idea of boundary critique is that the intimate
connection between boundary judgments and values exists, making it an ethical deci­
sion. Churchman W. C. [4] states that the more stakeholders the boundary includes, the
more inclusive and, therefore, ethical it becomes. However, it is acknowledged that sys­
tems thinking pursues the ideal of comprehensiveness but recognizes that it is unattain­
able. Midgley G., encourages actors to critically reflect on and make choices between
boundaries, and argues for challenging a consensus on boundaries by involving people
who may not be directly defined as affected or involved.
2
==PAGE:8==
Midgley G. emphasizes that the reflection on boundary judgments is an activity guided
by the use of theories and methods, crucial for understanding the ethical consequences
of various actions and the underlying ethical dimensions from which worldviews and per­
ceptions arise. Thus, in conclusion, he defines systemic intervention as purposeful
action by an agent to create change in relation to reflection on boundaries.
Transformation: Leverage points
The concept of Leverage Points was introduced by Meadows D. [5]. She posited that
twelve levels of interventions within a system exist, each exerting a significantly different
impact on the overall system. A change with a marginal effect involves altering constants,
parameters, or numbers (12th level), such as determining how much land should be de­
voted to biodiversity protection. Conversely, one of the most impactful levels of change
is the shift in the system’s paradigm (2nd level). This entails altering the shared ideas in
the minds of society, the deepest set of beliefs regarding how the world operates. For
instance, challenging the notion that economic growth is always beneficial.
Figure 1.1: Types of Leverage Points, proposed by D. Meadows. Source: [6]
1.1.2
Sustainable food systems
The UN Food and Agricultural Organization (FAO) defined a sustainable food system that
”delivers food security and nutrition for all in such a way that the economic, social and
3
==PAGE:9==
environmental bases to generate food security and nutrition for future generations are
not compromised” [7]. The United Nations called for major changes in agriculture and
food systems and established Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) which collectively
contribute to building a more sustainable, resilient, and equitable agri­food sector that
can meet the world’s food needs while preserving the environment and supporting the
livelihoods of farmers and rural communities [8]. Achieving the SDGs requires a trans­
formation of the global food system, making it not only more productive but also more
inclusive, particularly for poor and marginalized communities. This transformation should
prioritize environmental sustainability and resilience while ensuring healthy and nutritious
diets for everyone [7].
1.2
Background on design
1.2.1
Process models in design
The process model is a selected unit of analysis in this research. This section contains a
short description of the design process model(s).
The notion of a model is not novel. It is a natural human process to conceptualise internal
representations into more visual or tangible formulations. It could be illustrated by exam­
ples such as the Heliocentric universe model by Galileo or the Carnot model of the heat
engine. In the design field, before the formulation of design process models, it was con­
sidered that design ideas emerged somehow intuitively. Product concepts were thought
to change solely through changes in the product features or its production (e.g., the work
of Wögerbauer [9]). With the works of Kesselring (1942) [10], Leyer (1963) [11] and oth­
ers, the first indications of models defining the design process emerged. The attention
has shifted to handling the specific stages or steps of the design process itself [12].
Nowadays, the core general stages in the design process are often defined as analyse,
define, design, finalise, implement [13]. Alternative naming for the creative design pro­
cess can also be found in literature: the analysis of task, conceptual design, embodiment
design, and detailed design [14]. Other authors supplement these steps with two final
stages: Use and Closeout. Even if the titles of stages vary in the literature, the logic fol­
lowed is the same and has been consistent throughout decades and different areas of
design [13]. The comparisons of process models across disciplines [15] or composed
by different experts (e.g., academia, design studios) [13] also show that the majority of
processes follow the same process stage division.
Besides different stages, every process model has ”surrounding” components such as
input, output, stakeholders, tools, and principles/mindsets. These concepts are discussed
below.
Input
It is not common in the design literature to explicitly define the input to the design process.
However, relatively obvious resources such as expertise, available equipment, finances,
4
==PAGE:10==
or predefined requirements can be considered as inputs. The most prominent input is an
existing product, status, or paradigm that needs to be redesigned. According to obser­
vations in the literature, some designers approach their work from the perspective of the
initial status of the design object. Based on this, they tend to predetermine the intended
level of change in the design object and then choose the design activities accordingly [14].
This shows the importance of the initial input not only to the final output but also to the
design process itself.
In the same work, Howard J. T. and others compare numerous engineering design pro­
cess models and creative process models in cognitive psychology. A significant finding
among the models is the common need for information and the importance of its process­
ing in the initial process model phases. Later, the authors discuss that because of the
importance and lack of understanding of the role of information in the design process,
we must further explore the information sources as design inputs. Similarly, Takeda H.
presented knowledge as a premise in the iterative process of finding a design solution
and formulating requirements. In the case of ideal knowledge, it is possible to acquire a
solution instantly through defining an exhaustive list of specifications [16]. However, such
a case is not achievable in reality.
Output
In the field of engineering design practice, most types of models (e.g., problem­oriented,
solution­oriented) lead to clear outputs such as a new product, improvements in manu­
facturing, new service or other. When considering the General Design Theory, according
to Takeda H., the design process model runs until the design in development achieves all
pre­defined specifications [16]. This complete fulfilment of requirements could be consid­
ered a desired outcome. However, Takeda H. also states that no design is ever complete
and can always be improved, thus it is rather subjective. A number of researchers pro­
posed methods to classify different outcomes in design, which can also be assessed to
determine the level of novelty or creativity of the design outcomes [14].
Principles or mindsets
Inevitably, the design process model contains an inherent intention to guide a designer
towards a certain model of working. It could be framed as a principle or mindset that the
designer or stakeholders are expected to embody. An illustrative example could be taken
from the book “Design Thinking for Innovation”. The authors clearly state that Design
Thinking’s success depends on several principles, such as “innovation is made by humans
for humans”, “combining divergent and convergent thinking”, and “fail often and early”, just
to name a few [17].
Stakeholders
The designer and the object of design are pivotal in the design process [16]. Simultane­
ously, the design process is not executed in isolation. Different stakeholders are involved
in the design process at different stages, from users to other experts, such as engineer­
5
==PAGE:11==
ing, manufacturing, or marketing departments. However, analysis of numerous design
process models shows that they rarely represent the involvement of other disciplines [15].
Tools and Methods
Designers have consistently developed tools and techniques to support their design pro­
cesses. As outlined by the Danish Design Center, these tools often take on visual, intu­
itive, and even playful forms, like posters or boards, primarily used for fostering collabo­
ration. Moreover, they help teams in exploration or decision­making [18]. Various design
organizations, such as IDEO and Service Design Tools have created and shared their
design kits [19], [20]. This enables designers to enhance their sessions based on the
specific stage within the design process.
Assembling the design practice into simplified representative steps to formulate the pro­
cess model is beneficial. Process models allow to represent the design practices and
thus provide an opportunity for scientific testing of their claims [21]. Additionally, process
models are proven to be helpful in guiding novice designers in their first design projects
and their way of thinking in design [15]. However, studies show that experienced design­
ers demonstrate greater performance when they do not follow exact steps in the design
process [13].
1.3
Systemic design
Systems theory and design have long been interconnected, as evidenced by systems
thinker Ackoff R. A. (1993) [22], who defined systems thinking as a design process, and
Nelson H. (1994) [23], who viewed design as a systemic discipline. Although these per­
spectives were not necessarily adopted within their respective fields of science, they facil­
itated the exchange of knowledge and the exploration of each other’s methods and tech­
niques [24]. Systems thinking, seen as enabling the envisioning of the interaction and
workings of complex systems, and design thinking, considered to be more focused on the
outcome [25], appear to have joined forces due to their strengths in complexity compre­
hension and pragmatism, thus offering the opportunity to supplement one another.
This trend aligns with the observation that design has evolved to address problems in
complex social, environmental, and political issues [26]. In support of this evolution, sys­
temic design (SD) was separated from service design with the aim of addressing systemic
problems within advanced design domains: Organizational transformation (3.0) and So­
cial Transformation (4.0) [24]. Consequently, systemic design has become vital expertise
providing tools to tackle intricate scenarios practically while engaging stakeholders in ac­
tive collaboration [27]. Systemic Design is now considered applicable in challenges char­
acterized by “complexity, uniqueness, value conflict, and ambiguity over objectives” [28].
These complex challenges that we are facing are also referred to as wicked problems [29],
which the authors themselves define as “malignant”, “vicious”, “tricky”, and “aggressive”
(ibid., 160). Wicked problems are characterized as problems that are [30]:
6
==PAGE:12==
• Difficult to define, as everyone perceives them through different perspectives, making it
impossible to determine who is right or wrong.
• They have many interdependencies and are multi­causal, stemming from conflicting
goals or aims.
• There are no clear solutions, as interventions depend on the point of view, and the
true impact is challenging to trace. Rushing to ”fix” or ”solve” these problems can be
detrimental due to the possibility of making wrong assumptions.
• They are socially complex, involving changing behaviour and falling under the respon­
sibility of various organizations.
• Addressing them usually leads to unpredicted consequences due to the intricate rela­
tionships between elements in the system.
• They are often related to continuous policy failure.
• They are not stable; ironically, they evolve as one begins to address them.
According to Bijl­Brouwer van der M. [31], designerly practices are already evolving in
pursuit of creating change within the ambiguous and complex challenges that designers
are facing today. She identifies key differences observed in practice, highlighting how
systemic design distinguishes itself from conventional design practices [31]:
• Designers expand their natural tendency to explore the problem space to comprehend
the intricate connections of challenges within problem situations, doing so through map­
ping systems and utilizing other systems thinking tools.
• Designers navigate working with tensions between different stakeholders with conflicting
values as a result of shifting away from a user­centric focus to a broader system compre­
hension.
• Designers shift towards intervention designs of a systemic nature, focusing on building
human relationships and facilitating the transformation of mental models.
• The diverse nature of issues and interventions in the continuously evolving system re­
quires designers to commit to projects for long­term periods and build closer relationships
with involved stakeholders.
As evident, systemic design remains a dynamic and evolving field, continuously shaping
new methods, practices, and cultures through ongoing research [32]. The recognition of
systemic design as a field in the mainstream has grown through the annual Systemic De­
sign Symposium on Relating Systems Thinking and Design, organized by the Systemic
Design Association [33], throughout the past decade [34]. Universities involved in these
activities, as well as those with programs dedicated to systemic or systems­oriented de­
sign, such as the Oslo School of Architecture and Design, Ontario College of Art and
Design University in Toronto, and Politecnico di Torino in Italy, can also be considered
strong contributors to this development. However, concerns are arising about how to
proceed with the development within the field while maintaining its plurality and criticality
[34].
Numerous authors have contributed to guiding the development of the systemic design
7
==PAGE:13==
field by encouraging the monitoring and development of systemic design principles [31],
defining characteristics of the systemic design mindset, allowing exploration of the field
without the predominant mechanistic approach [28], or developing systemic design prin­
ciples that invite exploration of the methodological relationships between systems and
design theory [24].
In addition, Sevaldson B. [35] states that there is a misunderstanding in systemic design:
the assumption that systems theories or methodologies are applied to design. On the con­
trary, the two fields complement each other with the hope of fostering the emergence of
novel perspectives, theories, or processes. In his work ’What is Systemic Design? Prac­
tices Beyond Analyses and Modelling,’ to further support the emergence of the field, he
addresses core issues through eight points, clarifying what can and cannot be considered
systemic design. He shares his considerations by further describing SD characteristics
[35]:
Sevaldson B. asserts that modern ideas from systems thinking inspire systemic design
and that, as might be perceived, systems thinking does not solely rely on analytical pro­
cesses or hard data and logic.
Similarly to the earlier point in regards to complex issues, Sevaldson B. states that in
the context of wicked problems [29], the problem­solving mindset becomes obsolete [36]
as one solution in an interrelated system may create a problem elsewhere. With this
development, systemic design literature focuses on situations rather than problems.
He also addresses the large focus on topics of change and design within systems liter­
ature, emphasizing that the line between changing or designing systems is not precisely
clear.
He believes that systemic design is a praxeology (the science of human action [37]) due
to its comprehensive and ongoing exploration of skills, knowledge, and various elements
contributing to effective practices. Noteworthy contributors to this perspective include
Cross N. [38] and Dubberly H. [39]. The statement further underscores the significance
of reflection in action, as highlighted by Schon D. A. [40].
According to Sevaldson B., some perspectives regard design practice and design thinking
as inherently systemic activities. The utilization of visualizations for analyzing complex
data and the type of knowledge sharing facilitate a distinctive analysis that achieves new
levels of individual and team sense­making [41] [42], as well as sense­sharing [43]. This
approach leads to the adoption of co­design practices [44], [45].
Finally, systemic design is also ’questioning the divide between planning and execution’,
stating that the understanding will be reached that issues cannot be solved with a single
intervention and continuous systems change becomes a clear strategy of systemic de­
sign. The point also emphasizes the necessity of becoming accustomed to the idea that
interventions must be coordinated over time, as it may not be evident when the project
concludes [29].
The final point supports the systemic design principles of continuous adaptation and self­
organization, as found in the work of Lichtenstein B.B. and Plowman D.A. [46].
The
researchers emphasise the importance of encouraging the ongoing adaptation of sys­
tems while maintaining their intended purposes and objectives. This can be encouraged
through self­organization, in other words, agents that recombine themselves into work­
8
==PAGE:14==
ing arrangements, ultimately enhancing the functioning and performance of the complex
adaptive system [46].
In this emerging field of systemic design, researchers have identified three dominant ap­
proaches, as outlined by Bijl­Brouwer van der M. [47]:
1. Utilizing systemic approaches to inform design for systems through the use of systems
maps and network analysis [48];
2. Integrating design experience with systems theory to effect change within systems by
intervening at ’leverage points’. [49];
3. Engaging in continuous collective design processes from within the systems [50], [51];
1.4
Systemic design process models
While numerous systemic design frameworks and approaches have surfaced in the field,
they are not often formalized in scientific literature. The objective of this research was to
analyze, compare, and evaluate existing systemic design process models documented in
the scientific literature, resulting in the proposal of a new process model.
Building upon the preceding sections that defined boundary critique and transformation,
two principles were introduced to guide the assessment of existing process models and
facilitate the derivation of a new one:
The principle of Transformation: This involves acknowledging that the current system is
inadequate and unsustainable, and needs to change fundamentally. This entails altering
the mindset or worldviews that currently uphold the existing system.
The principle of Boundary Critique/Equity: This refers to a process that aims to en­
sure the greatest possible involvement of actors and their points of view, preventing the
marginalization of any issues and actors.
9
==PAGE:15==
Methods
2.1
Systematic literature review
The objective of this part of the research was to systematically review and analyse the
existing systemic design process models within the academic literature, to allow further
synthesis of characteristics and linkages between existing systemic design approaches.
This review focuses exclusively on academic literature found on the Scopus academic
database.
2.1.1
Data collection
Initial Research
The initial exploration of systemic design literature on the Scopus academic database
helped identify specific themes that frequently occurred in the found literature but did not
align with the scope of the research. For instance, numerous articles discussed computer
modelling solutions, which were considered inapplicable to the study’s scope as they did
not consider the processes led or experienced by humans, which is seen as essential
when discussing a systemic design process. Another example is the theme of product
manufacturing or optimization, which again focused on the work processes of machinery
or had a very narrow scope, covering the design of product(s) only. The latter can be
explained with reference to design domains that are relevant to systemic design. As Jones
H. P. argues in his work, the systemic design approach is situated in the advanced design
domains: Organizational transformation (3.0) and Social Transformation (4.0), whereas
the product design stage is considered to be a level lower [24].
These insights from the initial literature exploration on systemic design assisted in defining
the search thread for the Scopus database (Figure 2.1) and establishing the exclusion and
inclusion criteria for the research.
Inclusion and Exclusion Criteria
This section describes the eligibility criteria for the later stage of the review of the retrieved
material published on the Scopus academic database.
10
==PAGE:16==
Inclusion
The included literature should:
1. Represent a model focusing on the process of design that is divided into a set
of actions. It should be highlighted that literature should not be limited to design
discipline, as systemic design adopts a transdisciplinary approach.
Exclusion
The literature is excluded if:
1. The process model does not lead to interventions that are systemic.
2. The process model is not intended to be executed by humans. For example, ma­
chines or computer systems could aid humans in the process, however, the process
should not be designed to be carried out by these tools;
3. The “systematic design” term is used among the keywords. Systematic design
is a discipline that focuses on a methodological, structured approach to defining
design phases for product improvement. Hence, it is an individual design approach
that is also distinct from systemic design.
Search Thread
In the search thread systemic design and systems­oriented design [52] are treated as
synonyms. Systems­oriented design is a part of the systemic design field and was pop­
ularized through the course by the same name at The Oslo School of Architecture and
Design. The term process model is inevitably included as it is the unit of research anal­
ysis. Finally, ”design methodology” is included because relevant theoretical ideas and
methods might be discussed at the different stages of the process defined in the litera­
ture. In other words, there is a possibility of derivation of process steps even if they are
not made explicit.
The reasons for excluding the terms in the Scopus search can be found in the paragraph
on Initial Research above or the Exclusion section.
11
==PAGE:17==
Figure 2.1: Defined search thread in the Scopus database
Retrieved literature overview
The Scopus search provided 69 search results. The most common subject areas as­
signed to the literature were Engineering (32), Social Sciences (29), Computer Science
(18), Environmental Science (14), Business, Management and Accounting (12) and many
others. Most of the documents were articles (36) and conference papers (22). Single
publications occurred in years: 1984, 1995, 2003, 2006 and 2010 (these articles have
not passed the inclusion and exclusion criteria). Between 2011 to 2022 the publications
were more frequent, with most articles published in years between 2018 and 2022. This
characteristic substantiates the claim that systemic design is a new and emerging field.
Retrieved eligible literature
The abstracts of all 69 articles were analyzed. Articles were excluded based on an as­
sessment of the abstract, determining whether the study conformed to the predetermined
exclusion criteria. In instances where ambiguities arose concerning the satisfaction of the
inclusion criteria, the articles were retained for further comprehensive examination.
A total of 39 articles met at least one criterion of exclusion and were subsequently ex­
cluded from further analysis. Seventeen articles that appeared not to meet inclusion cri­
teria were further screened, and nine were excluded.
The second phase of our analysis involved a thorough examination of the remaining 21
articles to ascertain their adherence to the inclusion criteria. The articles were read fully.
Subsequently, six articles were discarded from the analysis for various reasons, such as a
lack of focus on the process or very specific coverage of algorithmic­based improvements
for information systems tools. Two articles were discarded due to the unavailability of an
English translation or the original text.

Data collection
Information on numerous specific topics was collected throughout the literature and doc­
umented in one Excel workbook (Appendix Fig. A.3). The list of topics included:
• Methodology of research
• Process model description, steps, principles/mindsets, input, output, actors/stake­
holders, tools
• Visuals related to the process model
• Process model strengths or weaknesses
• Area of application
• Systemic Intervention (Reflection on Boundary)
• Reference to other process model (if applicable)

==PAGE:20==
2.1.2
Data analysis
General Information about the Process Models
Seven of the articles presented in Table 2.1, (2, 3, 6, 7, 10, 11, 13, 14) provide process
models (at different levels of prescription), two of the articles (9, 12) describe the appli­
cation of the systemic design process (no explicit steps), three articles (1, 5, 8) present
systemic design tool(s) and finally, one article (4) defines systemic design principles.
Note that ”process model” will be used as a general term to define the systemic design
approach described in each analysed article, even though the level of process model
prescription differs among the literature.
More in­depth analysis of process model stages
To enhance the analysis of the process stages outlined in each process model found
in literature, the stage description, the tools employed in each stage, the objectives of
the stage, and the outcomes were mapped in a Miro board (Figure 2.3). Each row on
the board corresponds to an article in which the systemic design process is defined. The
order of the rows mirrors the arrangement in the table that lists the analyzed articles (Table
2.1). The columns represent the stages and their associated attributes. A consistent
color scheme: dark purple, light purple, light green, and dark green, has been employed
to highlight specific aspects of each stage. The dark purple cell serves as the stage
description, marking the beginning of a new stage. In the light purple cell, the tools utilized
in that stage are provided. Subsequently, the light green cells articulate the goal of the
stage. Finally, the fourth column is dedicated to the stage outcome (dark green). An
unpopulated cell indicates the absence of explicit information about the defined stages or
their respective attributes within the article.
This visual represents the different levels of available information within each analysed
article. This analysis allowed to understand the level or process model prescription.
15
==PAGE:21==
Figure 2.3: Prescribed steps in each article (also tools, goal and the output applicable to each
step) were mapped in the Miro board
Selection of main models based on comprehensiveness and
adherence to the principles
The process models found in the literature were evaluated in two diverse ways based on
different sets of criteria.
In both assessments, the process models were evaluated based on their alignment with
the Equity/Boundary critique principle. This criterion is considered crucial due to its
ethical nature and the perceived absence of relevant considerations in the analyzed pro­
cess models.
The second criterion is the level of detail of step prescription found in the analysed
models. It can be assessed on how evident are the steps and how elaborate are their de­
scriptions. In other words, how easy it would be for an unfamiliar practitioner to reproduce
the same process.
The third criterion is based on one of the goals of this research: to derive a process model
for sustainable transformation and thus adherence to the principle of transformation.
Different levels of system change be derived from analysis of the expected/ experienced/
designed outcome of each process model.
In Table A.2 (appendix) the arguments and scores given for each of the criteria are ex­
plained in detail per each process model.
Below are two assessments of process models according to the aforementioned criteria.
The results are visualized on the two separate matrix diagrams Figure 2.4 and Figure 2.5.
16
==PAGE:22==
Figure 2.4: Process Models evaluated in accordance to boundary critique and level of model
prescription
17
==PAGE:23==
Figure 2.5: Process Models evaluated in accordance to boundary critique and level of leverage
points
Both analyses revealed that the process model yielding the best performance according to
the criteria was the Design­Driven Conflict (DDC) model, identified as number 14. Follow­
ing closely as the second best­performing process model was number 3, documented in
literature as the application of Systemic Design Methodology (SD Methodology) within the
RETRACE project, focusing on regions transitioning towards a circular economy. It is im­
portant to note that Systemic Design Methodology has been discussed in various articles,
each taking different formats, such as describing a tool, application, or the process model
itself. The connection between these articles, all representing the same process model
(SD Methodology), was visually depicted in the matrix diagrams by being connected by
grey lines.
These two process models were chosen as the primary models for further analysis. Be­
yond their standing as the top models, they complemented each other. The DDC model
was highly prescriptive but lacked validation through empirical research. On the other
hand, the elaboration of steps and related details in the literature concerning the SD
Methodology, while inferior to the DDC, had seen application in numerous cases and
projects over extended periods. For the rest of the report, these process models are
referred to as main process models.
18
==PAGE:24==
Description of the main process models
Process Model: Systemic Design Methodology
This approach was developed by the Department of Architecture and Design at the Poly­
technic University of Turin, in collaboration with the ZERI foundation [64], in the early
2000s. The initial idea was to elevate the field of eco­design towards the direction of the
Blue and Circular Economy. Over the years, the SD methodology has become a sig­
nificant approach in academia, European projects such as RETRACE [65], [27], and in
collaborations with the industry (e.g., Agrindustria Tecco s.r.l.).
At the forefront of this methodology is the Holistic Diagnosis (HD) tool enabling the collec­
tion of qualitative and quantitative data allowing analysis between all elements in the sys­
tem. It helps involved actors perceive the complexity of the system with the help of visual
tools (infographics, gigamaps, etc.) and stimulates them to think holistically. Ultimately, it
encourages a horizontal dialogue between stakeholders and fosters synergies between
different involved disciplines [53], [27]. Therefore SD methodology considers building re­
lationships as the main prerequisite for finding innovative and anticipatory solutions [53]
or fostering new sustainable and resilient economic models [27] within complex systems.
Both articles present the SD Methodology while one focuses on the description of the HD
tool (one of the stages of the methodology) [53], another describes the practical appli­
cation of the SD Methodology in the RETRACE project, investigating the opportunities
for local and regional policies to integrate circular economy strategies, through effective
utilization of the territory resources [27]. According to SD methodology, restructuring re­
sources and thus relationships between actors result in resilient, self­maintaining systems
[53]. While, ultimately, SD methodology aims to encourage a paradigm shift by guiding
toward this new way of co­existing [53].
Process Model: Design­Driven conflict
The Design­Driven conflict (DDC) is founded on the consideration that it is essential to ex­
amine and redefine various types of relationships in order to intervene and create change
within complex adaptive systems [66]. It focuses on controversies, such as conflict or
disagreements as usually avoided and challenging forms of social relationships. The
methodology proposes that actively “constructing” such controversies can lead to the
emergence of new order, for example, alternations in the distribution of power [67]. By
constructing the author refers to ‘the capability of the social system in aggregating a net­
work of adaptive agents” at the edge of chaos [68]. Hence, the Design­Driven Conflict
methodology explores how controversies allow the emergence of new realities by desta­
bilizing the underlying social structures and the systemic ways of constructing conflict and
disagreement through the creation of the network of interactive agents [69], [63].
In this regard, the Design­Driven Conflict methodology also claims to address the per­
ceived limitations of existing systemic design intervention. It contends that collaborative
and participatory frameworks of design seeking mutual agreements and similarities of
viewpoints fail to satisfy the requisite variety principle of systemic design [24]. This princi­
ple requires sufficient representation in terms of actor diversity [70]. Due lack of a strategic
and more critical approach for intervention means that designers together with stakehold­
ers are able to synthesize the knowledge about the system but might fail pragmatically,
in delivering a strategic intervention to acquire the ‘voices’ of marginalized or unforeseen
19
==PAGE:25==
groups and comprehend the foundational narrative of the context [71]. Design­Driven con­
flict goes beyond the existing systemic design methodologies by including contradictory
voices [63].
To further analyze the Design­Driven Conflicts (DDC) methodology in this study, the fol­
lowing paragraph will explain the reasons for the author’s focus on controversies, as de­
scribed in his theoretical work on DDC [69].
While transformations within the system unfold gradually [69], controversies can acceler­
ate this process [72]. It is tightly related to conflict, facilitating the transmission of ”knowl­
edge and wisdom” [73], [74], [75], [76]. This should not be understood solely as acquiring
information but as shaping a new reality within social systems through collective under­
standing and interactions [69]. The bridge between the transmission of knowledge and
that reality is a change in ”deep culture and values” [69]. In system dynamics, connec­
tions between deep culture and social change are considered focal points in the shifting of
mindsets and paradigms within social systems [77], [5]. The premise of this methodology
is that ”conflict(s) of agents” work as internal stimuli activating fundamental changes in
these deep cultures and values through the transmission of higher­order knowledge [69].
However, the success of this depends highly on the utilization of conflict. This can be
achieved through a ”constructive form of conflict” which this methodology guides through
[69].
In contrast to SD Methodology, DDC is the prescriptive methodology that lacks empirical
evaluation.
Comparison of the main and the rest of the process models
Two principal process models were systematically analyzed and mapped, aligning them
with their corresponding stages. The Design­Driven Conflict methodology was repre­
sented in dark purple at the top, while the Systemic Design Methodology was indicated in
dark green at the bottom. Subsequently, the remaining models underwent further analysis
and were positioned on canvas in proximity to one of the main process models based on
their similarity. Only elements present in the analyzed models but not found in the main
models were incorporated into the map. This approach was followed in gathering data on
inputs, outputs, actors/stakeholders, and principles/mindsets.
The resulting map facilitated a comparative examination of the two main process models in
terms of the stages they prescribe. Additionally, it provided insights into elements present
in other process models that might be absent in the main models. It is crucial to note
that this information was subject to critical assessment, considering the known inferior
evaluation of these models compared to the main models. The mapping can be seen in
Figure 2.6, or greater resolution in the appendix Figure A.4, and the data collected on
other process model components can be found in Table A.3 (appendix).
20
==PAGE:26==
Figure 2.6: Comparison of the main process models and the rest of the process models
2.2
Primary qualitative data
2.2.1
Interviews with specialists
Three experts in the field of systemic design were interviewed online through the Microsoft
Teams application, with each interview lasting at least an hour. Two of these experts were
also the authors of the main process models analyzed in this study, bringing valuable
insights into their methodologies. The third expert is a member of the Expert Network
for Systemic Co­design (ESC), an eight­year­long project dedicated to exploring and ad­
vancing systemic co­design methods with the overarching goal of facilitating necessary
societal transitions [78]. Experts interviewed:
Carolina Giraldo Nohra, is a designer, researcher, and consultant specializing in Systemic
Design innovation processes. Currently works at Climate­KIC. Is the co­creator of the
Systemic Design methodology.
Moein Nedaei, a PhD student at the Universiteit Antwerpen in Design Sciences and author
of the Design­Driven Conflict methodology.
Bas van den Berg, an educational activist, researcher, futurist, and practitioner. He is a
21
==PAGE:27==
member of the Expert Network for Systemic Co­design.
The greater part of the interviews revolved around discussions on the methodologies de­
veloped by the authors and their practical experiences in applying them (if applicable). In
the final part of the conversations, the identified common stages within the methodologies
(at that time) were presented to the experts. They served as the foundational elements
of the process model in development. Furthermore, the principles of the process model:
Transformation, Equity, and Sustainability (at that time) were also introduced. The experts
were then prompted to share any relevant concepts, literature, theories, or other insights
that they believed would contribute to the ongoing development of the process model.
2.2.2
Interviews with AgriFoodTure roadmap developers
AgriFoodTure is a mission­driven partnership with a dedicated focus on accelerating the
green transition of the agri­food system in Denmark. The organization’s overarching am­
bition is to foster and lead collaboration across the entire value chain in this field [79]. The
AgriFoodTure naturally evolved from collaborative efforts in developing the ’Roadmap for
Sustainable Transformation of the Danish Agri­food System,’ which was published in May
2021 [80]. This document serves as a response to the ’Innovation Fund Denmark’s In­
nomission 3 call’ and stands as a collective achievement involving over 300 researchers
and experts from Danish universities and other academic institutions within the food sec­
tor.
In this project, semi­structured interviews were conducted with four key stakeholders en­
gaged in the development of the roadmap, aiming to gain deeper insights into how the
process of addressing such a complex challenge unfolded in practice.
2.2.3
Workshop
The workshop involved six participants: three AgriFoodTure representatives, one scien­
tist from the University of Copenhagen, and two representatives from Innovation Fund
Denmark. The workshop was divided into two parts. In the first, participants were asked
to map actors and their stakeholders, corresponding to stage 1 of the model at that time.
They were guided by specific questions: mapping stakeholders involved in the roadmap
development, mapping stakeholders not involved in the mapping development but con­
tributing to or hindering the implementation of the roadmap, and mapping stakeholders
that would bear the consequences of a successful or unsuccessful implementation of the
roadmap. The second exercise focused on mapping stakeholders according to different
types of power (stage 2 of the process model at that time). This involved identifying those
supporting or impeding the change in the system and recognizing actors with power over
others. Additionally, questions were posed regarding the origins of such power. To con­
clude, participants prioritized the actors they wished to invite in the process of revising the
roadmap.
22
==PAGE:28==
Figure 2.7: The template given to workshop participants depicting different stages of the process
model at the time
The primary goals were to assess participants’ ability to reflect on the importance of invit­
ing a more diverse group of stakeholders than in the initial roadmap development and
to determine if these exercises could stimulate deeper discussions or conflicts within the
group, allowing the discussion to reach the value levels of the system.
In the second part of the workshop, the systemic design process model was presented.
Participants received a canvas depicting different stages of the process model and were
prompted to reflect on their relevance to their specific case. Surveys were distributed at
the end of the workshop. The survey contained these questions:
1. Is systemic design applicable in your case?
2. To what extent did this workshop contribute to the planning of the roadmap revision?
3. Your key takeaways?
4. What would you have done differently if you were conducting the workshop?
The overarching goal was to evaluate the applicability of the systemic design process
model to the AgriFoodTure case, specifically in the context of revising the AgriFoodTure
roadmap.
Figure 2.8: On the left: the trial workshop with students and young professionals. Practice before
the workshop with AgriFoodTure. To the right: workshop with AgriFoodTure and other stakehold­
ers from Innovation Fund Denmark and the University of Copenhagen.

Coding of primary qualitative data
Interviews with SD experts
The expert interviews were recorded and simultaneously transcribed using the Microsoft
Teams application. Subsequently, the data underwent coding, where segments within the

tions, and other pertinent elements aligned with corresponding stages in the methodology.
The insights and practices shared by the experts were then visualized on the Miro board,
serving as inspiration for the iterative development of the process model (Figure 2.9).
Additionally, they played a crucial role in assessing the existing main process models.
Concepts or theories directly mentioned and relevant to the development of the process
model were separately listed and further investigated as part of the broader research pro­

and negative experiences of the process, and aspects of collaboration.
AgriFoodTure workshop
Data collection occurred through continuous note­taking during the entire workshop and
via a survey distributed at the workshop’s conclusion. The notes were analyzed with a
focus on identifying phrases that indicated a deeper level of reflection or reflections on

==PAGE:31==
Results
3.1
Systematic literature review
Comparison of two main process models: Systemic Design
Methodology and Design­Driven Conflict
In this section, a more in­depth examination of various stages in systemic design process
models will be presented through a comparison of the stages in the main process models.
As mentioned earlier, the Systemic Design Methodology (SDM) has been discussed in
several analyzed academic papers. Therefore, the analysis of SDM encompasses works
providing a generic description of SDM [53], [27],[57], the paper where the detailed de­
scription of the Holistic Diagnosis method (the first stage of SDM) is presented [53], and
the application of SDM in the RETRACE project, examined in [27]. The analysis of the
Design­Driven Conflict (DDC) methodology includes the theoretical framework of DDC
[69] and the prescribed DDC methodology [63].
Figure 3.1 provides a visual representation of the stage comparison between the two
methodologies. The ”sections” represent the division for comparing the process models
in the subsequent analysis.
Figure 3.1: Comparison of two main process models. The stages aligned horizontally are consid­
ered comparable. Interpretation of the stages and iterations are interpreted by the author of this
report. Full resolution image can be found in Appendix Fig. A.2
26
==PAGE:32==
Section 1
Detailed information on the stage(s) description and outcomes can be found in the ap­
pendix Table A.4.
In both main process models the first stage(s) encompasses establishing an ini­
tial understanding of the system context, however, employing distinct approaches.
Notably, both methodologies put a strong focus on resources within the system.
The Holistic Diagnosis method is the first stage of the SD Methodology starting with the
system mapping. This entails an examination of material resource flows and the resulting
interplay of actors related to those resources. It includes the analysis of a broader context
such as geographical, demographic, and cultural factors. In essence, it encourages an
overview of the existing relationships between components and processes. This mapping
is conducted by researchers engaged in desk and field research. The information is ulti­
mately visualized in the gigamaps (a popular tool within systemic design [58] and a format
for presenting extensive quantitative and qualitative data). Finally, the map is presented
to other actors for sharing their perspectives.
In contrast, the DDC model, during the ”Context mapping” stage, focuses on mapping both
human and non­human actors to comprehend the resources available to them. In the sec­
ond stage, ”Analysis,” DDC enhances understanding by probing into the reasons for ex­
isting power dynamics within the system. It specifies power relationships between actors,
particularly those in conflict or marginalized. Additionally, the influences from neighbour­
ing systems and other factors, including historical and geographical elements are also
analysed in this stage.
The primary objective of both process models remains the same: to establish a compre­
hensive understanding of the system in question. Moreover, the DDC model takes an
extra step by delving into the underlying causes of the intricate power relationships in the
current system to identify relevant actors.
Interestingly, the mapping in SDM is conducted by researchers and is given to actors in
the end to hear other perspectives on the analysis. While it seems that the mapping is
conducted with actors within the DDC methodology ”discussion between actors that can
further facilitate the unfolding of the unknown side of the context” [69].
Section 2
Detailed information on the stage(s) description and outcomes can be found in the ap­
pendix Table A.5.
The fundamental concept that appears to be shared between the DDC stage ”Synthesis”
and the SDM stage ”Definition of problems and leverages for change” (or ”Good Practices
selection” as titled in the RETRACE project, where SDM was applied in a real­life setting)
is the identification of focal points for instigating change.
However, the methods employed in these stages are hardly comparable.
In the SDM Methodology, emphasis is placed on context analysis, stemming from the ini­
tial activity. Collaborating with participants, researchers analyze connections and impacts
27
==PAGE:33==
to identify potentialities and criticalities of future scenarios. Challenges are approached
as levers for change, shaping how the system can be designed and initiated. In the RE­
TRACE project, researchers also engage actors to share and select good policy practices,
scrutinizing their strengths and weaknesses. These findings contribute to the subsequent
design of a new system.
In the DDC methodology, methods such as storytelling are employed to comprehend the
challenges faced by actors in conflicts or disagreements. This stage is also dedicated
to transparent and inclusive discussions for marginalized actors, where hidden assump­
tions and unshared knowledge can be exchanged among participants. The objective is
to uncover commonalities within the system’s actors.
Hence, during the SDM process, the focus is on the components of the board sys­
tem, whereas in the DDC methodology, individual experiences and fundamental
perspectives are analyzed.
Section 3
Detailed information on the stage(s) description and outcomes can be found in the ap­
pendix Table A.6.
In this stage, intervention occurs in both process models, guiding the actors toward
an intended future.
The general description of SDM indicates that a new system is designed in this stage,
where new value is generated through resource utilization and relationship building. How­
ever, the application of SDM in the RETRACE project reveals a more guiding intervention
rather than a definitive one. The outcome of this stage is the formulation of regional ac­
tion plans presented as ”future­oriented visions,” identifying policy gaps and proposing
various possibilities for interventions for actors.
In the DDC process model, this stage is pivotal, serving as the methodology’s foundation,
where translation points are established. The translation aims to unite actors with conflict­
ing views or interests, facilitating the construction of an ideal network of allies. Through
multiple translation moments, the intention is to shift the learning paradigm of the context,
transitioning from one’s understanding of the situation to an ideal state. The responsibility
falls on the designer to create these passage points, offering alternatives for actors or
enabling them to explore new realities. The prescription defines this stage as an inter­
vention and prototyping phase in the conventional design process, wherein the designer
intervenes in the system through the use of artefacts, services, or other elements that
may require prototyping and testing. Translation is an opportunistic approach, never a
completed accomplishment; it may fail under certain conditions. In other words, the result
of translation is not an ultimate solution; instead, it establishes a condition or situation for
learning and adaptation in response to a problematic situation.
In comparison, the SDM methodology appears to afford more freedom for actors
to define their activities, whereas interventions in the DDC methodology are prede­
fined by a designer.
In addition, they both focus on fundamentally different goals. The SDM model aims
28
==PAGE:34==
to change the system by altering its elements and relationships, while the DDC method­
ology intends to bring about a change in worldviews or the mindsets of the actors, which
could be more closely related to altering the function or purpose of the system.
Section 4
Detailed information on the stage(s) description and outcomes can be found in the ap­
pendix Table A.7.
In this stage of the SDM process model, a preliminary evaluation of the interven­
tion(s) is conducted. This assessment may encompass economic, environmental, and
social factors. For instance, in the RETRACE project, the outcome was the determination
of success or the identification of total/partial failure factors in activity implementation.
Even though in the ”Translation” stage of the DDC methodology it is discussed that the
intervention for translation in the system creates opportunities for learning, there is no
exploration of how this learning could be evaluated. It might be viewed as a more natural
process within the system rather than as an analytical procedure.
Consequently, there is no apparent alignment between the SDM and DDC method­
ologies in this section.
Section 5
Detailed information on the stage(s) description and outcomes can be found in the ap­
pendix Table A.8.
In the final ”Scale­up” stage of the DDC methodology, exploration centers on how the new
allies and the acquired knowledge they now share can be scaled up within the broader
community. The proposal suggests that the design’s capacity to conceptualize and ide­
alize the future could contribute to utilizing the narrative, norms, and values emerging
in prior stages to construct an aspirational platform. It could be considered as design­
ing a catalyst for the further spread of new mindsets and worldviews within the broader
community.
The SDM methodology contains two final stages: ”Implementation” and ”Results Anal­
ysis and Feedback.” The goal during these stages is to implement successful interven­
tions/systems and ultimately measure whether they deliver the expected outcome: an
autopoietic system that fosters a paradigm shift. In the practical implementation of SDM,
the stage labelled ”Action Plan Implementation” appears to encompass both the ”Imple­
mentation” and ”Results Analysis and Feedback” stages of the SD methodology. It delves
into how the outcomes of initial interventions address, inform and support future activities
within the system. Described as an ”actionable and proactive” mindset of SD which inte­
grates with a foresight vision. This can be viewed as an iterative process, moving from
implementation to learning, and then to the ability to anticipate and modify future strate­
gies.
It could be concluded, that both methodologies envision a change in the system
occurring through a gradual learning process.
29
==PAGE:35==
The DDC attempts to accelerate the change process by targeting relevant actors (antag­
onistic or marginalized) and creating conditions for knowledge transmission within them.
That is how the paradigm shift is achieved within the group of allies in prior steps. Finally,
the goal of the methodology in its final stage is to foster conditions for the further spread
of these new ideas and worldviews. In SDM, the change within the system unfolds as
a gradual process where interventions inform subsequent interventions. This continuous
learning approach facilitates a deeper understanding of the system and enables modifi­
cations, ultimately guiding the system to become autopoietic—capable of independently
maintaining itself.
Systemic design process model components: principles/mind­
sets, stakeholders/actors, input, output, tools
In this section, a summary of the other process model components identified within pro­
cess models in academic literature will be presented. These components have been
gathered and categorized into four sections for a more systematic analysis. The seg­
mentation aligns with the process model assessment depicted in Figure 2.5, organized
according to their placement within the four quadrants of the matrix. The full description
of all the components can be found in the Table A.3 (appendix).
Principles/ Mindsets
An apparent pattern emerges from the principles or mindsets articulated within the top­
performing process models (found among QI, QII, and QIII). All these models encompass
the connectedness and relationships among stakeholders in the process, underscoring
the power of collaboration and co­creation. This could be concluded with the statement
in [61] ”The virtuous cooperation between the actors becomes the system itself.”
Stakeholders/Actors
Nearly all process models emphasise the significance of multidisciplinarity and advocate
for broadening the identification of stakeholders to enhance their diversity. However, only
the DDC process model addresses the involvement of marginalized actors, which aligns
directly with the boundary critique/equity principle. Additionally, emphasis is placed on
the involvement of stakeholders in the process and the integration of their ideas or per­
spectives. Notably, one process model [60] distinguishes itself by focusing on analyzing
users’ wants or preferences, aligning more closely with a user­centric design approach
rather than the systemic design approach.
Input
Within the two best­performing process models, the input to the process model, or the
starting point, is described as the ”problematic context” or ”given scenario.” This aligns
with the Sevaldson B. statement, emphasizing that in systemic design, the focus is on
situations rather than isolated problems [35]. Another model specifies the requirements
for the systemic design team, while one of the primary models (also among the best­
30
==PAGE:36==
performing) initiates the process with stakeholder identification and engagement. Explicit
mention of inputs in other analyzed scientific literature proved challenging to find, likely
due to the infrequent prescriptive nature of the analyzed models, which more often were
descriptions of project experiences.
Output
Diversity characterizes the outputs among all process models. For example, the highest­
ranked model claims to facilitate the mindset and paradigm shift at the end of the process.
The most frequently occurring theme among outputs relates to the establishment of re­
lationships between stakeholders and the improvement of levels of stakeholder cooper­
ation. Social or collective learning also emerges as a recurrent outcome of the systemic
design process.
Tools
The tools were not systematically analyzed and compared across all models. This deci­
sion was driven by a focus on the rationale behind the process stages and their outcomes,
rather than the means to achieve them. Additionally, tools identified within the stages of
process models were not specifically designed for the process or explicitly identified as
systemic design tools. Instead, they were often borrowed from other fields, such as de­
sign or transition sciences. While the relevance of these tools is not dismissed, a thorough
analysis was avoided until their use is justified, such as their applicability in the derived
process model.
3.2
Action research
Theories, literature or methods proposed by the systemic de­
sign experts
Carolina Giraldo Nohra
She perceived the Equity principle in relation to human relationships, the environment,
and the process of connecting these elements. She emphasized the need to avoid
making this principle too basic. Carolina also delved into stakeholder involvement, noting
that the Quadruple Helix model is often practical, however, it depends on the local territory
and one’s connections. Regarding the sustainability principle, she urged exploring the
value creation theory. Also in the interview, she referred to Mariana Mazzucato’s book
”Value of Everything” as a resource helping to avoid generalizing terms like ”circularity”
or ”sustainability”. Carolina also suggested looking into the Theory of Change, Portfolio
of Innovation approach, and MEL framework for implementation and learning stages
within the methodology, enabling project monitoring and long­term strategic planning.
Moein Nedaei
Moein focused on different systemic design approaches. He distinguished between ”system­
31
==PAGE:37==
oriented design” and ”design­oriented system”, with the former involving intervention at
various system levels and the latter being more strategic, focusing on the right actors,
space, or competency for intervention. Essentially, the focus is on the power to tran­
scend the paradigm. Both approaches, according to Moein, start with understanding the
existing system and identifying leverage points, after which they diverge.
Moein further discussed the concepts of transformation and transition, characterizing
one as providing a deep change and the other as ”more change”. He also mentioned
the concept of transformability as an attribute of social systems. He recommended
looking into the work of B. Walker on resiliency, adaptability, and transformability [81].
Additionally, Moein highlighted F. Avelino’s work on power dynamics, emphasizing its
relevance to sustainability, which might be interesting to this research.
Bas van den Berg
The expert initially recommended exploring transition design literature. Regarding the
sustainability principle, which was one of the principles at the time, Bas van den Berg
suggested ”easy” considerations such as Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs), the
Donut model, or Life Cycle Assessments (LCA). Regarding the equity principle, he
stressed the significance of understanding who/what are the various stakeholders in­
volved. They should be represented in the process, either through personas, employing
role­play with other actors, or directly inviting them to participate in the process. Bas van
den Berg underscored the importance of obtaining an overview of the mindsets and
worldviews of individuals involved in the system to foster transformative learning.
To illustrate, he proposed introducing a nano level when considering the traditional micro,
meso, macro perspectives. This nano level allows engagement with the individual’s
worldview. According to him, transition design tools can be instrumental in addressing
these aspects.
Almost all expert feedback was taken into consideration. The mentioned works and spe­
cific suggestions were thoroughly reviewed, supplemented by brief desk research on other
shared concepts or theories. The literature that appeared more closely aligned with the
existing process model concept at the time received greater attention. However, the Sus­
tainable Development Goals (SDGs), the Donut Model, or Life Cycle Assessments (LCAs)
were excluded due to presumed lack of relevance. Additionally, Mariana Mazzucato’s
book on the ”Value of Everything” was omitted due to time constraints. To what extent the
concepts were integrated in the process model design is explained in the section on the
background of the proposed process model stages (section 4.2).
The expert reflections and feedback were mapped against the stages of the systemic
design process in Figure 3.2, under the sections ”Practices proposed by experts for PM”
and ”Good practices employed by experts”.
How the previous roadmap process dealt with each aspect or
step of systemic design
The process through which the roadmap was developed bears little resemblance to a
systemic design process. As described by a participant in the workshops, ”this was a
highly political process”, where heads of universities gathered before the official call of
32
==PAGE:38==
the roadmap to determine the tracks and the overall direction. The participants faced the
challenging task of representing their own agendas, making it unlikely that comprehensive
considerations were given beyond their personal expertise and their field of science.
It is known that achieving consensus among the leading actors was challenging due to
conflicting interests. Power dynamics likely influenced decisions in the room, but the
opportunity to challenge underlying power structures was lost as the process unfolded
behind closed doors.
Consequently, it can be concluded that the roadmap primarily serves the interests of those
in the room. This stands in contrast to the bottom­up collaborative process proposed by
systemic design and the process model derived in this research. In addition, it strongly
opposes the equity considerations that are addressed in this research.
Regarding the practical development of the roadmap, it was mentioned that at least one
of the four tracks took a value chain approach, allowing for a ”more interdisciplinary per­
spective”. However, it is known that stakeholders outside of universities were not actively
involved in the process. The roadmap was developed solely by academia, aside from a
few editors from other public organizations. Other institutions were included only for the
update on the process, strategically to gain buy­in. However, the organizations in this
meeting were allowed to share ideas.
While an extraordinary number of researchers were invited to share their expertise, the
decision on what to include or exclude was made by the same small group of experts.
This led to many professionals feeling unacknowledged for their work.
Much of this criticism can be directed at the organization that initiated the roadmap, which,
according to research, allocated only a few months for a project of such complexity. This
top­down decision restricts the possibility of a thorough process involving multiple stake­
holders that requires thoughtful preparation, organization, and substantial time to conduct.
However, despite the time constraints, interviewees, while critical of the limited time, were
also positive about the amount of work produced. Some interviewees believed that the
results might have been similar even with more time.
The positive aspects of the roadmap in terms of systemic design include its long­term
vision “we wanted to make a roadmap that could be used for many years” and its role as
a guiding document. However, it is crucial for the roadmap to ensure that the proposed
directions are of a systemic nature.
The past roadmap development process was mapped against the stages of the systemic
design process in Figure 3.2, under the section ”Past process of the roadmap develop­
ment”.
Expectations for the next process of revising the AgriFoodTure
roadmap
The workshop provided a platform for participants to delve into the systemic design ap­
proach and the proposed process model. The central focus was to understand the appli­
cability of the process model to the agri­food sector. However, a key challenge emerged
when two participants could only attend the second part of the workshop, resulting in the
33
==PAGE:39==
restructuring of the agenda. Due to time constraints, the process model was presented in
a presentation format, limiting practical engagement with each stage for all participants.
Fortunately, the other four participants had briefly covered activities from the first two
stages of the process model earlier on. As a result, the understanding of different parts
of the process model heavily relied on practical experiences and the presenter’s ability to
convey complex concepts in a short time.
Furthermore, given the power dynamics in the room, there was a need to accommodate
participants updating each other on their progress, addressing the lack of recent collabo­
ration.
Overall, participants responded very positively to the systemic design approach and the
process model. Considerations for each stage of the process model are visually repre­
sented in Figure 3.2, section ”AgriFoodTure Workshop”, with further reflection available
in Appendix A.0.3. Participants found the ”Power Dynamics Analysis” and ”Envisioning”
stages crucial in revising the roadmap. One participant viewed the Power Dynamics activ­
ity as an opportunity to foster inclusivity and give individuals ”a voice.” Some participants
were familiar with and had practised the method of ”Theory of Change.” They were not
impacted and did not particularly agree with the criticism of it from the systemic design
perspective. In addition, they agreed with the importance of developing a learning culture,
with an invitation to cultivate it already offered from one organization to another during the
workshop.
Participants described the workshop as ”inspirational” and an ”eye­opener,” noting that
it sparked excellent discussions, input, and a new way of thinking, offering diverse per­
spectives. All participants considered the systemic design approach relevant to the Agri­
FoodTure case, with one answering ”maybe” to this question. Several participants also
requested materials from the workshop to be sent to them afterwards. While the pro­
cess generated space for new ideas, and participants expressed enthusiasm to explore
it further, there was insufficient time to plan concrete actions.
Finally, at the end of the workshop, one participant approached, stating, ”I used to work
with system designers, and I heard bits and pieces of this, but I think the way you put it
together made a lot of sense”.
