Analysis
4.1
Process models for systemic design
4.1.1
Contrasting existing process models to the principles
and good practices recommended by specialists
Design­Driven Conflict process model
The process model is analyzed, taking into account the principles defined in this re­
search, systemic design principles defined by Jones H. P., the author’s own reflection,
gaps identified from other process models, learnings from interviews with systemic de­
sign experts, and expert feedback on the DDC process model available in the article
describing the DDC methodology [63].
Principle of Transformation
The DDC process strongly asserts its ability to adhere to the ”Transformation” principle.
The process model was strategically crafted to bring about a paradigm shift within the
existing system, which is considered one of the highest leverage points by Meadows
D. [5]. This model operates on the premise of identifying pathways to understand the
underlying mindsets or worldviews of the involved actors, aiming to subsequently alter
them. According to experts, comprehending these mindsets is a crucial prerequisite for
system transformation or initiating transformative learning.
However, it’s important to note that the creator of the DDC methodology has openly
acknowledged the challenge of establishing a space for individuals to engage in con­
versations about their fundamental values. Additionally, criticism from other experts
has been expressed towards the proclaimed goal of a paradigm shift. It is claimed to be
difficult to rely on or define within the time frame of a project.
36
==PAGE:42==
In response, alternative viewpoints have been proposed by various experts, suggesting
a focus on purposefulness in terms of both structural considerations and collaborative
aspects among intervening participants. Some have advocated for identifying sub­steps
that could serve as facilitators in the process towards achieving the goal of the paradigm
shift.
Principle of Equity
The DDC process model could be considered the most equitable among analyzed pro­
cess models, as it is the only model that delves into the identification of marginalized
groups, specifically individuals lacking power or those oppressed by others within the
existing system. However, the methodology description lacks sufficient data to explain
precisely how these groups can be identified or whether there is encouragement for their
inclusion in the process. Indeed, one expert emphasized the need for actors to have
more agency in the process.
While it may be perceived as the most inclusive process among the analyzed models,
a contradictory claim arose during the interview with the author: ”We don’t need people
or even non­actors. We only need narratives.” Consequently, the author mentioned ex­
perimenting with how this process could be supported by AI. The notion of excluding the
human element from the process might be seen as contradictory to making the process
diverse or inclusive, as it works against the cooperative nature of systemic design.
Hierarchical intervention
As stated by the author of the Design­Driven Conflict methodology, the creation of pas­
sage points, or pathways for changing mutually agreed concerns, is a highly hierarchical
and structural process. In this approach, the designer/researcher takes on the role of
mapping the context and translating those elements into narratives, providing actors
with a guide to establishing new alignments. The author acknowledges that this ap­
proach is somewhat ”stupid” and expresses difficulty in making it more “democratic”.
Furthermore, it contradicts the bottom­up approach in the systemic design field, where
interventions are ideally developed collaboratively within co­creative environments that
include diverse groups of actors.
Furthermore, one expert highlighted the importance of approaching systemic design
with epistemic humility, avoiding viewing the designer as someone who can alone fix
the issue.
Evaluation/ Lack of Feedback coordination
Compared to other process models found in systemic design literature, the DDC model
37
==PAGE:43==
skips a step of evaluation. Feedback coordination, as defined by Jones H. P. [24], is a
systemic design principle that emphasizes the importance of feedback loops in achiev­
ing the desired output and, ultimately, the desired effect. While the DDC acknowledges
that a desired shift in mental models may require multiple translation interventions, as
illustrated by the iteration loop in Figure 3.1, there is no mechanism for evaluating the
success of these translations. Moreover, success in the DDC model is dependent on
the success of facilitating the formation of allies, but there is no mechanism for under­
standing the negatives or positives of these processes to enable improvements.
Unlike other process models that evaluate interventions through different perspectives
of various contexts or actors, the DDC interventions are designed by the designer/re­
searcher without an attempt to consider them from a different point of view.
Power dynamics
The analysis of power within the system is a unique aspect of the methodology, particu­
larly relevant within the field of transition studies. According to one of the experts, it is a
crucial part of systemic design, yet unfortunately taboo concept in our society. This can
be attributed, in large part, to our cultural inclination toward consensus, due to which
we tend to avoid discussions on power. The DDC process model, however, challenges
this norm.
Usability
According to the experts, the DDC methodology has been perceived as highly complex
and may prove overwhelming for non­researchers, including practitioners or design stu­
dents. The extensive amount of time taken to analyze this process model for this re­
search reinforces this perspective. In a sense, this complexity makes the methodology
even more reliant on the facilitator or researcher, diminishing the power allocated to the
actors and limiting the opportunities for it to be used independently by practitioners.
38
==PAGE:44==
Figure 4.1: Analysis of the Design­Driven Conflict process model
==PAGE:45==
Systemic Design Methodology process model
The process model is analyzed, taking into account the principles defined in this re­
search, systemic design principles defined by Jones H. P., the author’s own reflection,
gaps identified from other process models, learnings from interviews with systemic de­
sign experts, and strengths and weaknesses pointed out among articles on Systemic
Design methodology.
Principle of Transformation
One of the descriptions of the Systemic Design Methodology states that, ultimately, the
aim of the process is to achieve a paradigm shift [53]. This is defined as a desired
final outcome, but unlike the DDC methodology, it does not seem to prescribe strategic
actions allowing for the specific attainment of that level of complexity. According to
experts, understanding the underlying causes of the system is considered a prerequisite
for that level of transformation.
However, the SDM expert mentioned in the interview attempts to ”change people’s vi­
sions” in a participatory setting, as well as efforts to create ”vulnerability” within the
group. Importantly, these elements are not explicitly mentioned in the methodology lit­
erature. Moreover, it remains unclear how this knowledge would be applied. While one
might assume it could be used in designing the ideal system, in practice, interventions
in the system (in SDM) often target the levels of elements or relationships rather than
the mindsets of the actors.
The discussion in the methodology leans towards making the system self­maintaining
by changing the management of resources and relationships. This concept could be
associated with the power to self­organize the system structure, identified as one of the
highest leverage points according to Meadows D. However, it is still lower in priority
than changing the system goals or the mindsets and paradigms from which the system
evolves.
Principle of Equity
The prescriptive literature on Systemic Design Methodology only emphasizes the impor­
tance of multidisciplinarity. In the practical application of the SDM process, the focus is
on inviting the necessary stakeholders, however, solely based on the mutuality principle.
Unfortunately, there is no consideration given to stakeholders who might be marginal­
ized, underrepresented, or could bring unique perspectives, even though the context
involves policy­making, which is an outcome with significant societal implications.
Additionally, it is noteworthy that in the initial stages, the mapping of the system is carried
40
==PAGE:46==
out by a smaller group of researchers who define the system’s boundaries. However, it
is important to mention that this mapping is later opened for stakeholder feedback, thus
might be open for further inclusions of other issues or actors.
In conclusion, there is a noticeable absence of evidence indicating special or targeted
considerations aimed at making this process more equitable.
Intervention
Interventions proposed in the system appear to be broad, providing actors with the
agency to create their actions. It functions more as a guidance tool, in comparison to
the hierarchical intervention led by a researcher/designer in the DDC model. However,
this co­creation approach also grants more agency to the actors and promotes a com­
mon benefit by enabling them ”to think holistically and to overcome divisions between
different sectors and roles, pursuing shared objectives” [27].
Furthermore, it emphasizes the importance of involving all stakeholders not only in the
design of the main planning stages but also in each of the subsequent actions. Other­
wise, as observed in practice, achieving active participation from actors who were not
involved in the early stages would become challenging.
41
==PAGE:47==
Figure 4.2: Analysis of the Systemic Design Methodology process model
==PAGE:48==
4.1.2
Other common gaps in the current models
Continuous Adaptation
Counting on the system to behave consistently after the intervention is a common error
in systemic design. Monitoring the system’s behaviour over time allows for maintaining
a system that thrives in a dynamic environment [24], [54], [82].
However, the DDC methodology does not explicitly discuss how, at the end of the pro­
cess, the effect on the system is expected to be monitored, measured, or evaluated to
determine if the created effect is desirable. The ”solution” is rather scaled­up and is left
without further iteration.
A similar weakness can be observed in the SDM. In interviews, the expert and co­author
of the SD methodology focused on its final stages, stating they were the weakest part of
the methodology. Her current experience in the field allows her to reflect on the pitfalls
in the prescribed process. She suggests methods such as the Theory of Change or the
MEL framework, which not only allow for monitoring but also facilitate communication
of intervention expectations to the clients. Which, she believes, is essential to be able
to take action in real life.
Being able to monitor system behaviour and creating opportunities for continuous adap­
tation would allow the system to maintain its preferred purpose and objectives [24].
Stakeholder diversity
The selection and involvement of stakeholders were discussed in the previous section.
The process models primarily focus on more obvious stakeholders relevant in the exist­
ing system. However, one expert also emphasized the inclusion of ”other stakeholders,”
such as future generations. In Ulrich’s terms, his Critical Heuristics methodology allows
for reflecting on system boundaries, involving those affected, including ”those who can­
not speak for themselves, such as future generations and non­human nature” [83]. The
incorporation of such considerations would significantly support the principle of Equity.
Idealization
Idealization stands out as a systemic design principle, revolving around the identification
of a desirable state and defining the conditions to achieve it [24]. Ackoff L. R. suggests
that the most effective way to shape the future is by constantly evolving the present, and
while guided by ultimate values, moving towards the ”ideal” [22]. Hence, idealization
serves as a “future­finding process” [24].
In the DDC model, the designer’s ability to idealize the future is discussed as a skill that
43
==PAGE:49==
can mobilize new narratives and norms, facilitating the scaling­up of a paradigm shift.
In the SDM application in the RETRACE project, the Regional Action Plans are referred
to as ”Future­oriented visions”. However, these process models do not detail specific
methods for envisioning or describing how the visions for the future are achieved. Ad­
ditionally, in the DDC model, the mindset shift aims to be achieved through moments
of translation, where the path is envisioned and designed by the designer. It raises the
question of whether one person can define ”the way” for all.
It is noteworthy that Walker B., in literature recommended by experts, suggests that
deliberate transformation involves exploring ways of living when the existing system
is unsustainable [81]. He advocates for scenario planning as a process to success­
fully navigate such futures, expanding imagination and opportunities, and focusing on
the plausibility of created scenarios [84]. Evaluating what is reasonable and probable
heavily depends on the knowledge actors possess at the time of design. During a con­
ference on designing for transition and transformation, Dorst K. expressed concern that
through ”futuring” people project their experiences. However, through the use of design­
erly methods and creativity, other possibilities can be comprehended and novel futures
imagined [85]. Hence, different forms of futuring are considered an essential part of the
transformation field.
Moreover, during the interviews, experts themselves highlighted the importance of the
theory of value creation or transition design, which incorporates ”co­created visions for
transition,” employing methods such as scenario development, future­casting, or spec­
ulative design [86].
In conclusion, even though idealization could be perceived as an important element
in systemic design or design for transformation, these existing process models lack
prescription regarding the envisioning of the future. Consequently, it is challenging to
determine how significant it was perceived by the process model authors themselves.

4.2
Background of the proposed process model stages
This section marks the beginning of the presentation of the new process model, which
has been formulated based on the insights gathered during the research. Within this
section, the explanations behind the determination of the prescription for each stage of
the process model are presented. The subsequent section (Section 4.3) will provide
an in­depth description of each stage of the process model, covering aspects such as
goals, activities, inputs, outputs, stakeholders, and tools. Both sections are organized
according to the stages outlined in the derived process model
The process model was derived based on:
• initially defined principles of Transformation and Equity (Boundary critique);
• insights gained from the examination of process models found in academic litera­
ture, including process stages/steps and components (input, output, etc.);
• the evaluation of the main process models and their gaps and strengths identified;
• interviews with systemic design experts and their best practices;
• and additional literature that was explored following the feedback from systemic
design experts.
The primary goal of this research was to derive the process model intended for trans­
formative and equitable change in the system.
Note: All parties involved should be informed about the intention to investigate and de­
sign a fundamental shift in the systems. Agreement with the intention is not necessary,
as it may pose the risk of overlooking important stakeholders in the process, potentially
those with significant power and no desire to change.
The initial focus of the process model is to unveil the underlying worldviews of the actors
involved in the process, as this has been recognized as a crucial activity for enabling
transformation. During the initial two stages, ”System Mapping” and ”Power Dynam­
ics Analysis”, a space is established for stakeholders to delve into the depths of their
mindsets and challenge each other’s perspectives.
4.2.1
System mapping
The objective of the ”System Mapping” stage is to map the actors, their resources, and
the contextual environment of the current situation. This process facilitates an under­
standing of how stakeholders involved in the process perceive the existing situation.
46
==PAGE:52==
Furthermore, it serves as an artifact where stakeholders’ perspectives become visible
and can be challenged by other participants during the system mapping.
A significant aspect of the mapping process is dedicated to identifying resources and
their distribution among actors. The emphasis on resources is justified by their connec­
tions to power dynamics within systems. Power is linked to a stakeholder’s ability to
mobilize resources [87], [69]. The insights on resources will assist in the subsequent
stage of the process where power dynamics in the system will be analysed.
The DDC methodology suggests mapping actors using the multi­actor map model, ini­
tially proposed by Pestoff V. as the ’welfare mix’ [88] and later adopted by Avelino F.
[87]. In the DDC framework, the author proposes mapping to perceive the resources
stakeholders possess based on their roles and responsibilities within clusters. However,
in this process model, the tool is modified under the assumption that mere perception
is insufficient to support actor discussions.
The multi­actor diagram divides actor mapping into four sectors: Market, State, Com­
munity, and ’Third Sector’ (comprising NGOs, volunteers, etc.) [87]. The triangle rep­
resenting the multi­actor diagram is positioned at the center of the canvas (see canvas
Fig.4.4. In the map, an area for resources is added, visually presenting resources to all
participating actors. Moreover, visualizing resources has the potential to expose dispar­
ities in resource possession and power relations. For instance, in Western societies, the
State­market sectors tend to dominate [87], which is likely to be visible in the mapping.
That can serve as a visual prompt for actors’ reflection. (Note: Different characteristics
may be identified for applications in other locations.)
To encourage actors to broaden their perspectives beyond the four conventional sec­
tors, the canvas includes areas designated for ”other actors” and ”affected actors.” The
concept of affected actors is drawn from considerations by Ulrich W. [83], who devised
a set of questions to explore the system’s boundaries. The aim is for participating ac­
tors to pinpoint additional stakeholders who might become involved in the process in
the future or whose interests should be rightly considered.
In contrast to main process models like SD Methodology and Design­driven conflict,
where mappings are typically constructed based on field research, interviews, or desk
research, this process is carried out collaboratively with the actors. For the same rea­
son, it is not intended to achieve the same level of comprehensiveness as the gigamaps
commonly used in systemic design [58]. The mapping in this methodology serves the
purpose of fostering a dialogic discussion among actors and facilitating their under­
standing of the surrounding system. Furthermore, the mapping plays a crucial role in
uncovering elements that will support the ”Power Dynamics Analysis”, the subsequent
stage of the process model.
The activities are prescribed in the process model stage description in section 4.3.1.
47
==PAGE:53==
4.2.2
Power Dynamics Analysis
Importance of Power dynamics analysis
When delving into studies related to social change and innovation, a key focal point
is the examination of power dynamics among actors within the system. Despite the
numerous ways to understand and define power, one of the few consensuses among
theorists is that power relations undergo change when the social context is changing.
Therefore, ’changing power relations form an inevitable dimension of social change and
innovation’ [89]. Consequently, the transformation of social relations can be regarded
as the principal characteristic of change [90]. Hence, one of the primary objectives in
this stage is the mapping and comprehension of power dynamics with the ultimate
aim of modifying them later on.
Antagonistic and marginalized actors
As proposed in the DDC model, unveiling the controversies within the system (conflicts
or disagreements) and actively engaging with and ’constructing’ them in the process
can expedite the pace of change within the system. Controversies, often avoided and
overlooked as a form of social relations, play a crucial role in fostering critical discus­
sions among actors. ”Controversies facilitate the emergence of a new reality through
destabilizing underlying social structures” [69] (further details are available in the de­
scription of the Design­Driven Conflict process model in the section 2.1.2). To initiate
this discussion in this stage, our initial focus is on identifying antagonistic actors, those
engaged in conflicting relationships.
Lastly, it is crucial to identify actors who might be marginalized and excluded from the
process. Identifying actors oppressed by the power dynamics of others can serve as an
indicator of those who are potentially absent or underrepresented in the context.
Power dynamics analysis
The analysis of power involves examining its nature and its potential to either promote
or hinder change within the existing system. Avelino F. categorizes power into three
types: reinforcing, transformative, and innovative [91]. Understanding the type of
power actors exert within the system can provide insights into whether they contribute
to the existing problematic situation or possess the incentives and resources to alter the
system’s functioning.
48
==PAGE:54==
The acknowledgement that the complex problematic situation exists, allows familiar ac­
tors to identify and communicate these structures within the system. In addition, as
highlighted by an expert evaluating the DDC model, ”Ingredients such as resources,
actors, or power dynamics are available in almost every context. Hence, regardless
of the type of problem we are addressing, the method has the capacity to be used in
broader and different contexts” [63].
Three different types of power enable us to assess the overall landscape, revealing
who hinders or facilitates change in the system. However, in the methodology, it is also
important to identify antagonistic and marginalized actors.
Avelino F. has outlined seven distinct types of power contestations that describe various
approaches and notions of power among different theorists [89]. In this methodology,
our specific focus centers around the ’Quantity < > Quality’ contestation, within which
nine different power relations and dynamics can be mapped. Avelino F. has introduced
an empirical set of questions designed to systematically explore the role of power in
change and innovation from diverse perspectives (ibid.). Some of these questions are
incorporated into this process model, guiding participants to understand which actors
are in antagonistic relationships and identify those oppressed by others, a concept that
Avelino F. terms as ”one­sided dependency”.
Integration of iceberg model for further depth of analysis
Drawing from the workshop experience with Agrifoodture, it was observed that even
without extensive facilitation, actors were able to uncover underlying mental models
within the system. However, to ensure this depth of consideration in other participatory
settings, the canvas is expanded with elements from the Iceberg diagram [92]. Fre­
quently mentioned by experts as a tool enabling a deeper dive into underlying causes,
it is integrated as a prompt to foster these deeper discussions.
Exerting a form of power in the system or over others can be perceived as a behavioural
pattern (the 2nd layer in the iceberg diagram). Consequently, two deeper layers, ”System
Structure” and ”Mental Models” are incorporated, each accompanied by supporting
questions: ”What influences these patterns?” and ”What values, assumptions, beliefs
shape the system?”. The template can be seen in Fig. 4.5
Following these activities, the goal is to achieve a comprehensive understanding of the
system and foster extensive discussions that unveil the underlying mental models of the
system. Ultimately, the actors should collaboratively discuss what elements in the
system need to change and what aspects should be retained or improved.
The activities are prescribed in the process model stage description in section 4.3.2.
49
==PAGE:55==
4.2.3
Envisioning
When confronting wicked problems characterized by ambiguity, lack of ownership, and
no obvious pathways for change, it may seem that the existing state has reached a
stalemate. A similar sense of gloominess was experienced after the first two stages
in the workshop with AgriFoodTure, where participants, upon identifying the underlying
values of the system, felt powerless and ”doomed.”
Envisioning is considered a means to overcome this sense of incapacity. ”Visioning is
crucial; it creates spaces for discussion and debate about alternative futures and ways
of being, and it requires us to suspend disbelief and forget how things are now and
wonder about how things could be” [93]. The systemic design principle of idealization
and the power of envisioning, along with their significance in systemic design and design
for transformation, has been covered in the section of ”Idealization” 4.1.2.
Futures Literacy emerges as a practical method to avoid paralysis by exploring the fu­
ture, enabling individuals to examine the present with a different mindset, as proposed
in the Relating Systems Thinking and Design (RSD12) Symposium [94]. UNESCO rec­
ognizes Futures Literacy as one of the most important skills of the 21st century [95],
while Dorst K. highlights its potential to enhance creativity in the field of futuring [85].
Hence, the Futures Literacy Laboratory process, one of the techniques for working
with people’s anticipatory assumptions, is prescribed in this process model.
After collectively undergoing this process, actors will have formulated an image of the
future and a common vision that guides them towards the desired ideal. This can be
considered the purpose of the future system. According to the Purpose Finding sys­
temic design principle defined by Jones P., the purpose is not something to be identi­
fied; rather, it needs to be established through mutual agreement [24]. Even if the goals,
means, or ends of the system change, a system with a defined purpose will continue
moving in the same direction [96]. Furthermore, the unity, identity, and functioning of
the organization depend on the common pursuit of this purpose [97]. Therefore, defin­
ing this purpose, or in other words, a future vision that everyone is working towards, is
of crucial importance.
Additionally, sub­steps, or in the context of transition design, the visions for transition
on how to create that future should be established [86]. The elements of the system that
need to be changed, retained, or improved, identified in the previous stages, can serve
as a starting point for creating these visions. These visions for transition will provide the
means to evaluate the designed interventions against the desired future state [98].
”Embracing the uncertainty of the future allows you to take ownership of something that
is not yet there” [94]. The concept of ownership has been a recurring theme in the
process of developing the process model. One of the experts highlighted that in the
systemic design process, the concept of ownership needs to be rethought as it cannot
50
==PAGE:56==
solely rest in the hands of the designer. During the workshop with AgriFoodTure, par­
ticipants expressed concerns about the lack of ownership among actors in the mission
and the absence of a common goal as the reasons for stagnation in the process.
In this stage, these concerns may be overcome. Co­creating the vision of the future
together might foster unity among actors, and defining a common purpose could con­
tribute to the establishment of a coalition moving strongly together towards the same
ideal. Here, everyone has a role to play and a vision to realize.
The activities are prescribed in the process model stage description in section 4.3.3.
4.2.4
Ex­ante evaluation
To ensure that visions for transition are more equitable, it is essential to encourage the
exploration of system boundaries. In the practice of co­creating ideal service systems,
Midgley G. utilizes Critical Systems Heuristics to facilitate this process and enable the
exploration of potentially overlooked considerations. Critical Systems Heuristics, devel­
oped by Ulrich W., serves as a framework with twelve questions designed to provoke
thoughts and encourage reflection about the boundaries set in the system [83]. This
method is known to be used and understood by both professionals and common peo­
ple, as affirmed by Ulrich W. and demonstrated in practice by Midgley G. [99]. These
questions on how the future ought to be can be employed in this process to allow actors
to broaden their considerations, contemplate the potential inclusion of new actors, or
explore how the marginalization of actors or issues can be overcome.
As Meadows D. stated, in a world where the future is unforeseeable and the effects
one creates in the system are only temporary, individuals must ”expose their mental
models to the open air” [100]. Engaging in systemic intervention in relation to reflection
on boundaries is a way to expand one’s mental models. The mental flexibility to reframe
boundaries is an essential skill in designing for an ever­changing world.
The activities are prescribed in the process model stage description in section 4.3.4.
4.2.5
Adaptive Learning
Adaptive Implementation encompasses both implementation and learning. Interven­
tions in the system are seen as opportunities to generate knowledge that contributes
to building an understanding of the system and, in turn, altering our mental models.
Recognizing that a single intervention cannot fix the system and that tracking the inter­
vention’s impact becomes challenging within the ever­changing, dynamic system, the
primary objective is to learn how to support the continuous adaptation of the system
51
==PAGE:57==
while upholding the established purpose [82]. The overarching goal is to move the en­
tire system toward an improved state [22].
Some experts in the interviews and in the AgriFoodTure workshop strongly advocated
for the theory of change as a practice to understand the evolution of interventions. How­
ever, the theory of change is also under scrutiny for its linear nature, which fails to ad­
dress the challenges of complexity [101]. Despite existing criticism, some argue that
the theory’s limitations may be attributed to culture, values, and mindsets inherent in
our society and that the theory itself has been designed for systemic challenges, thus
its applicability should not be dismissed [102].
In response to criticism regarding linearity, the concept of Systemic Theories of Change
(SToC) was developed by merging leverage analysis and the theory of change. SToC
is based on the similarity of the causal structure of causal loop diagrams and theories
of change [101].
While one of the experts described the theory of change as ”a very didactical tool” in this
process model, the use of Systemic Theories of Change aims to offer practical tools to
actors for designing and managing change strategies.
Ultimately, the intention of this stage in the process model was to close the gap identi­
fied in the two main process models ­ the lack of strategies to support the continuous
adaptation of the systems. While Systemic Theories of Change (SToC) enable better
monitoring, management and evaluation of interventions, the tools or methods to learn
from them might still be insufficient in the field of systemic design.
The activities are prescribed in the process model stage description in section 4.3.5.
4.3
Proposed model prescription
4.3.1
System mapping
• Goal: Mapping the context of a problematic situation
• Input: Complex problematic situation
• Output: System map
• Stakeholders: A wide range of actors (ideally that are part of the system and
affected by the system), a facilitator (recommended)
• Tools: Adapted multi­actor map
The background explaining the design of this activity can be found in section 4.2.1.
52
==PAGE:58==
The process begins by introducing the actors to the complex problematic situation, likely
brought about by the client or a group of actors intending to solve the problem.
Participants can be encouraged to start the process by presenting their roles in the
context of the situation, avoiding delving too deeply into their understanding of the other
components. Following this, the mapping phase unfolds the complex situation, where
participants are allowed to further articulate their views on the system.
The first and second stages, ”System mapping” and ”Power dynamics analysis”, aim to
ignite discussion among participants, bringing to light the underlying mindsets or world­
views of the actors. Participants are not obliged to reach a consensus, instead, they are
encouraged to share their perspectives. The facilitator plays a crucial role in stimulating
discussion and ensuring the documentation of perceptions. Additionally, the facilita­
tor must balance actor participation, especially for those who may appear oppressed
and are less vocal. The facilitator may also assume the role of representing actors not
directly involved in the process.
The complex problematic situation is mapped on the canvas such as in Figure 4.4.
1. Identify and Map Resources:
Start by pinpointing and mapping the resources present in our complex situation. These
resources may encompass mental, monetary, artifactual, natural resources, materials,
laws, tasks, and other elements. Resources play a crucial role in the power dynamics
of our system, as the ability to mobilize them can be seen as the ”power” of the actor.
Mapping resources first sets the stage for defining actors later. Experience shows that
it’s challenging for participants to map resources after actors have already been identi­
fied.
2. Identify and Map Actors:
Now, shift to mapping the actors. The canvas is divided into five sections of actor types
to ease mapping: Market, State, Community, ’Third Sector’ (including NGOs, volun­
teers, etc.), and Other/Affected actors. The facilitator(s) should ensure that participants
consider not only those actively involved but also those affected by the situation. Partici­
pants may view this section as less important, as people in the room are often individuals
with more influence in the analyzed context.
3. Specify Relationships:
Simultaneously with mapping actors, specify relationships between different elements.
This includes relationships between actors and relationships between actors and re­
53
==PAGE:59==
sources. These connections define the overall performance of the system [22]. De­
scribe these connections between elements briefly—this isn’t a Causal Loop Diagram
(where identifying negative or positive polarities is common), but rather a practical way
to communicate and document relationships.
4. Expand with Broader Landscape:
Add elements to the Broader Landscape section.
Here, include factors like spatio­
temporal elements, political, geographical, cultural, social, industrial aspects, or any­
thing relevant to actors or their resources. (For instance, organic food as a resource
can be seen to grow due to trends of having a healthier diet).
5. Define Actionable Points:
Finally, articulate the phenomena in the system that you want to eliminate, change, or
retain. Participants should keep in mind that the goal is to move away from the exist­
ing problematic situation. Hence, elements to eliminate could be considered those that
sustain the current state, while elements to retain are the ones guiding the system to­
ward a new direction. Treat this as a conclusive action, creating a ”bank” of actionable
”directions” decided upon by the actors.
54
==PAGE:60==
Figure 4.4: Sequence of activities in stage 1 of the process model
55
==PAGE:61==
4.3.2
Power Dynamics Analysis
• Goal: Analyse the power dynamics within the system, uncover underlying mental
models supporting the existing problematic situation
• Input: System map
• Output: Underlying mental models supporting the existing problematic situation
• Stakeholders: A wide range of actors (ideally that are part of the system and
affected by the system), a facilitator (recommended)
• Tools: Empirical questions by Avelino F. [89], a custom tool representing three
sorts of power by Avelino F. [91], and the adapted Ice­berg model [92]
The background explaining the design of this activity can be found in section 4.2.2.
The actors will likely alternate between the ”System Mapping” and ”Power Dynamics”
stages. Ideally, the stages can be conducted together or in a closely connected time
frame, as both mapping activities can inform each other. Hence, participants should
refer to the mapping of actors in the previous stage. Given our aim to transform the sys­
tem by substantially altering and improving the current problematic situation, we need
to identify actors working towards facilitating this process and those hindering it by at­
tempting to maintain the status quo.
1. Map Actors on the Power Wheel:
Initiate the process by mapping identified factors onto the ”power wheel” as in Fig.4.5.
Reinforcing power identifies actors working against change, while Innovative and Trans­
formative powers define those supporting change with unique fostering abilities. Explain
Avelino’s three power types (Reinforcing, Innovative, Transformative) to the participants.
Note that explanations of the terms should be tailored according to participants’ age and
expertise (or other considerations).
Here are the definitions proposed by Avelino F. [89]:
Reinforcing Power: Involves the ability to strengthen and perpetuate existing struc­
tures and institutions.
Innovative Power: Characterized by the capability to generate new resources, em­
powering actors to reduce reliance on established power structures.
Transformative Power: Pertains to the capacity of actors to challenge, modify, and
replace structures and institutions. This involves the development or renewal of le­
gal frameworks, physical infrastructure, economic paradigms, or religious ideologies.
It does not entail creating entirely novel entities but rather significantly challenging, al­
56
==PAGE:62==
tering, or replacing existing institutions by reconfiguring, transposing, and reinventing
specific elements.
2. Analyze Power Relationships:
Following the initial mapping, carefully analyze the power relationships between actors.
All relationships are important and should be mapped. However, it is crucial to facilitate
participants to recognize conflicts and potentially marginalized actors. To assist with
that identification, apply Avelino’s predefined questions [89]:
How and to what extent are different kinds of power exercised for/against change, by
and over whom?
How and to what extent are various resources mobilized for change?
What different types of resource­based power (e.g., ideological, economic, political) are
being exercised in processes of change, how, and by whom?
How do power relations/dynamics manifest in change (e.g., cooperation, competition,
co­existence, synergy, antagonism)?
3. Facilitate Power Dynamics Discussions:
The facilitator has a pivotal role in discussions. Pay special attention to actors in conflict,
encouraging them to share perspectives and challenge each other’s mindsets. Empha­
size actors with ”no power” (mapped in the centre of the canvas) or those overpowered
by other actors (according to the mapped relationships). Ensure their representation,
whether through involving them in active participation more proactively (if actors are in
the room) or taking up a role of the actor(s) (if actors are absent).
In addition, if essential actors identified within the conflicting and overpowered cate­
gories are not involved in the current process, it may be worthwhile to explore their
inclusion in upcoming stages.
4. Reflection on System Structure:
Transition to the ”System Structure” circle. Encourage actors to reflect on factors in­
fluencing actors’ behaviour. Given the challenge of articulating such aspects, the fa­
cilitator could prompt participants to share stories from their experiences. There are
several questions that allow provoking a deeper discussion without the facilitator taking
a stance. For example [103]:
57
==PAGE:63==
Thank you for sharing. Can you expand on this idea?
What does that mean?
What would the opposite of this look like?
Can this idea be defined only as good/bad?
Together, define the structures shaping actor behaviour for a deeper understanding.
5. Reflection on Mental Models:
Prompt participants to reflect on identified structures. What assumptions, beliefs, and
values do people hold about the system? What beliefs keep the system in place? Foster
the discussion among actors.
Additional prompting questions that would allow reaching the depth of this level of re­
flection and also relate to the dynamics of power:
Where does such power (of an actor) come from?
What motivates actors to adopt antagonistic behaviours?
Why do some actors depend on others, and how does this dependency impact the pro­
cess of change?
6. Define Actionable Points:
These steps lead participants through a systematic exploration of power dynamics within
the system, fostering meaningful insights. As in the previous activity, prompt participants
to determine what in the system needs to change and what could remain unchanged.
It is crucial to emphasize the level of ”Mental models” identified by participants. Chang­
ing the mental models of the system would bring about the most significant change,
aligning with the intended goal of the process model to facilitate transformation. Add
considerations to the ”bank” of ”actionable points.”
58
==PAGE:64==
Figure 4.5: Sequence of activities in stage 1 of the process model
59
==PAGE:65==
4.3.3
Envisioning
• Goal: Envision the future and define the visions for transition
• Input: Identified elements of the system that needs to be changed, retained, or
improved
• Output: Visions for transition
• Stakeholders: A wide range of actors (ideally that are part of the system and
affected by the system), a professional facilitator (required to prepare in advance)
• Tools: Futures Literacy Laboratory technique [103], [104]
The background explaining the design of this activity can be found in section 4.2.3.
Out of all the stages, this one relies the most on the facilitator’s experience. Neverthe­
less, the Futures Literacy Laboratory technique, upon which this stage is built, offers
various resources to equip facilitators for the process [103], [104]. The technique aims
to engage with people’s anticipatory assumptions and reduce potential stagnation re­
sulting from addressing wicked issues and potential gloominess after identifying the
underlying causes of the existing problematic situation in the previous stages.
The process is founded on the Future Literacy Laboratory technique [104]. and has
been customized slightly to suit the current stage of the process model.
1. Introduction to the Methodology:
In the first stage, the facilitator introduces participants to the methodology and outlines
the day’s upcoming activities. Since participants have already progressed through the
initial two phases in the process model, they have acquired an understanding of the
existing problematic situation. However, a brief review of past activities, especially phe­
nomena of the system that actors identified for retention, improvement, or elimination,
could be conducted.
As preparation before the session, participants may be requested to choose an image
reflecting their vision of the future in 2050. This image, along with their thoughts or feel­
ings, can be shared as a warm­up and a reflection on past activities.
2. Imagining Desirable Futures:
During this session, participants envision a future for the year 2050. They have the
opportunity to articulate their visions, liberating their imagination from the constraints of
realism and expressing hopes, ideals, and aspirations.
The facilitator encourages participants to take a moment to reflect and discuss the future
60
==PAGE:66==
in the present tense. Participants write down their ideas, share them with the group, and
ultimately present their desirable future.
Here are examples of guiding questions, assuming the case relates to a desirable food
system in 2050:
• How would the media describe the current food system? What would the news head­
lines be?
• What and how do we consume?
• Which individuals, groups, and organizations sustain the system?
• What political, geopolitical, and economic forces shape the system?
• What social and technological changes influence the system?
• Which routines, ideas, or myths drive the system?
3. Imagining Probable Futures:
In this session, participants construct a likely scenario for 2050, outlining practical and
realistic expectations. The goal is to identify preconceptions and mental barriers.
Similar to the previous session, the facilitator encourages participants to pause, think,
and talk about the future in the present tense. The participants write down ideas, share
them, and present their probable future. The guiding questions from the previous ses­
sion can continue to guide this process.
4. Reframing the Future:
Before this session, the facilitator(s) prepares an alternative scenario for the system
in question, in 2050. Ideally, the scenario would be different than the ones already
analyzed in the past sessions, as it intends to challenge the anticipatory assumptions
of the participants.
At the beginning of the activity, the facilitator introduces the alternative scenario, urging
participants to envision an unforeseen future. The exercise aims to uncover potential
opportunities and obstacles often overlooked. Participants then imagine societies, lives,
and traditions based on this new reality, using the present tense.
Guiding questions for reframing the scenario include:
• How do people meet their basic needs in this unfamiliar scenario?
61
==PAGE:67==
• What surprises or concerns you about this scenario?
• Can you imagine news headlines for the reframed scenario?
Participants generate ideas individually, group them, and present their scenarios. Other
participants are encouraged to ask questions allowing the presenting actor to have a
deeper exploration of their scenario.
The session concludes with participants reflecting on how the reframed scenario alters
their understanding of the current system.
5. Framing the Future:
Encouraged by the facilitator, actors should utilize insights gained from the previous
activity to collaboratively frame the vision of an ideal future. This vision becomes the
overarching purpose toward which the actors will strive collectively.
While the purpose is intended to be grand and broad, participants should define sub­
steps or visions for transition. They can be considered as pathways leading toward
the ideal future. The previously identified elements of the complex problematic situation
that need to change, be improved, or be eliminated can serve as valuable input or
inspiration in developing these visions. These visions will inevitably be interrelated, tar­
geting the problematic complex system in question. However, they also create a chance
for actors to choose the pathway they believe they can contribute to the most. These
visions for transition will serve as benchmarks, allowing the evaluation of designed in­
terventions against the desired future state.
4.3.4
Ex­ante evaluation
• Goal: Reflecting on the boundaries of the system
• Input: Visions for transition
• Output: Revised visions for transition
• Stakeholders: A wide range of actors (ideally that are part of the system and
affected by the system)
• Tools: Critical Systems Heuristics [83]
The background explaining the design of this activity can be found in section 4.2.4.
As the visions for transition are developed, this step encourages participants to engage
in reflection by explicitly stating their judgments that uncover their understanding. This
involves utilizing a table of questions developed by Ulrich W. [83], [105]. The decision
62
==PAGE:68==
to incorporate these questions into the process is driven by three key reasons [105]:
1. Promoting Clarity on Internal Assumptions:
The questions serve as a structured framework for sense­making, by prompting partici­
pants to articulate their thoughts and judgments. By making their understanding explicit,
individuals can enhance clarity in communication and contribute to a more transparent
and holistic understanding of the relevant issues:
Values and motivations (how we perceive situations and attempt to improve them).
Power structures (what is identified as a problem and potential solutions)
The knowledge (what is an appropriate information, including experience and skills)
The moral foundation (expecting third parties (concerned stakeholders not directly in­
volved) to address the consequences of our actions or inactions in a given situation)
2. Unfolding multiple perspectives:
Ulrich W. suggests that misunderstandings often stem from diverse framings of situ­
ations, a concept known in systems science as using different reference systems. It
is possible that we’re not consciously aware of these differing perspectives, making it
challenging to systematically analyze and address them. Utilizing Ulrich’s questions
provides a systematic approach for participants to delve into various aspects of their
understanding.
3. Guiding Thoughtful Reflection:
The questions act as prompts that guide participants in thoughtfully reflecting on their
visions for transition. The key point is that no intervention will ever be suitable for every­
one. Therefore, it is essential to systematically consider what aspects our judgments
and designs might overlook and consistently evaluate them from different perspectives.
Ultimately it contributes to a more robust and well­informed decision­making process.
63
==PAGE:69==
Figure 4.6: Critical Systems Heuristics Questions (Adapted from Ulrich 1996, p. 44) [105]
The template can serve as a platform for actors to express their answers by attaching
sticky notes to the available questions. Its purpose is to foster dialogue, establish align­
ments regarding visions for transformation, and challenge the mental boundaries that
shape our systems, potentially expanding them.
By encouraging participants to leave their responses in the form of sticky notes, the
template facilitates a dynamic exchange of perspectives. This interactive approach pro­
motes a shared understanding of assumptions, values, and motivations, leading to con­
structive discussions about the broader picture.
The aim is to stimulate conversations that transcend individual viewpoints, encouraging
participants to challenge and reassess their mental frameworks. Through this pro­
cess, the template becomes a boundary object for transformative discussions, paving
the way for a more comprehensive and inclusive vision of the systems in ques­
64
==PAGE:70==
tion.
4.3.5
Adaptive Learning
• Goal: Developing interventions and fostering learning opportunities
• Input: Revised visions for transition
• Output: Interventions in the system and opportunities for learning
• Stakeholders: Causal loop diagrams expert(s) (recommended), a wide range of
actors (ideally that are part of the system and affected by the system), a facilitator
(recommended)
• Tools: Causal Loop Diagram, Leverage analysis, Systemic Theories of Change
[101]
The background explaining the design of this activity can be found in section 4.2.5.
In this process, actors actively engage in analyzing the intricate relationships within each
vision for transition, aiming to formulate targeted interventions in the system in order to
learn more about the system.
1. Developing Causal Loop Diagrams:
The process begins by mapping causal loop diagrams of the existing problematic sit­
uation in relation to the proposed visions for transition. For example, if the vision is
to empower small organic farmers within the agri­food system, various elements such
as infrastructure, laws, incentives, and land should be considered and mapped. The
collaborative creation of causal loop diagrams allows actors to share knowledge and
enhance the comprehensiveness of the mapping process [106].
65
==PAGE:71==
Figure 4.7: Example of the causal loop diagram. The example is taken from a fragment of the
causal loop diagram conducted in the workshop recorded in [106]
2. Selecting the Phenomenon:
Secondly, a phenomenon aligned with the vision must be selected within the causal loop
diagram. Assuming the same vision, the phenomenon could be ”Economic viability of
organic farming.” This phenomenon must align with the vision and will work as an input
for modelling.
3. Expert­Driven Modelling:
In this activity, an expert in causal loop diagrams is involved. They use input from the
co­development session of causal loop diagrams to create a computer model of the
causal loop diagram to conduct the leverage analysis. Following the steps outlined in
[101], through modelling, various aspects of the system represented in a causal loop
diagram are identified.
These aspects include the lists of:
Opportunities, which, if improved, have the potential to have the most positive impact
66
==PAGE:72==
on the whole system (potential high­leverage systemic interventions);
Leverage points, representing aspects that are easy to change and can create a change
in the system (levers to target with interventions);
Measurable indicators, offering ways to track systemic change (signals);
and barriers or bottlenecks hindering change.
The lists of found elements should be reviewed and assessed within the broader team
to remove impractical or unrealistic elements, which are likely to arise from computer
modelling.
4. Crafting Systemic Theories of Change
The list of these elements: opportunities, leverages, signals, and bottlenecks, assists
the team in creating systemic theories of change. These are the pathways between
potential interventions (opportunities) and the goal (phenomenon) identified in stage 2.
The path should be created to target the levers and signals while deliberately avoiding
the bottlenecks. This activity will require a lot of interpretation from the team as some
elements can be considered to have different system features, for instance, to be both
a leverage and a bottleneck.
Visualizing the results from the leverage analysis can also aid in the process.
Here’s an example of the visualized diagram for the systemic theory of change:
67
==PAGE:73==
Figure 4.8: Diagram for the systemic theory of change. The example is taken from [101]
(Note: Every intervention intended for introduction into the system should be looped
back to the ”Ex­ante evaluation” stage (stage 4 of the process model) and assessed
accordingly. This ensures that interventions are rigorously evaluated before implemen­
tation, fostering a continuous and adaptive approach of the systemic design process.)
4.b) Resource­Conscious Exploration
Even if there are no resources to involve an expert to conduct the leverage point anal­
ysis and to extract elements for developing systemic theories of change, the activity of
exploring the system by creating the causal loop diagram is crucial. It allows for analyz­
ing the intricate relationships in the complex system and understanding the interrelation
between the different visions for transitions. Ultimately, it allows for a better decision­
making process.
Systemic theories of change (as well as causal loop diagrams) enable us to represent
the dynamics of systems, interpret their complexity, and formulate plans for improving
the system by understanding how changes connect to the main goals. This approach
allows us to predict what to expect and, consequently, learn from interventions and re­
flect on them.
5. Continuous Learning and Adaptation
68
==PAGE:74==
All coalitions of actors responsible for different visions in the system should agree on
regular points for reflection, also called learning cycles [107], with the purpose of ex­
changing knowledge and observations on emerging patterns of the system caused by
interventions. The reflection on the intervention is conducted against the intended vi­
sions for transition.
While mapping the systemic theories of change might have allowed determining what
can be expected with the intervention, it is even more relevant to gather information
about the patterns that emerged unexpectedly.
Actors from all tracks could share their learnings about emerging themes. These could
be done by sharing their observations and writing them down on the sticky notes in the
participatory session, and later using a method such as an affinity diagram to look for
overarching patterns among all future pathways.
Are there emerging patterns in the system that no one expected? How do they change
our understanding of the system? How does that influence future interventions? Have
the mental models of the actors changed? How do these learnings influence the vision
of the future in itself?
This continuous process of learning and adaptation is integral to systemic design
and the ability to create purposeful change in the complex system.
69
==PAGE:75==
Discussion
5.0.1
Limitations of the Field
The scientific literature on systemic design remains limited, with a scarcity of compre­
hensive systemic design process models. Many existing models lack sufficient detail
or prescription, often presenting only their application in real­life cases or reflections on
such applications. This deficiency results in a lack of explanation regarding the funda­
mental rationale behind the design of specific process stages which is fundamental in
understanding the process models.
The majority of scientific literature in this domain originates from the research group at
Politecnico di Torino, predominantly focusing on a singular systemic design approach
and associated strategies. The second most influential source of inspiration is systemic
design principles defined by Jones H. P. or even specific design stages proposed in his
work. Consequently, the current landscape of systemic design process models can be
perceived as lacking diversity, given its concentrated sources of influence. Furthermore,
a substantial portion of these models, including main process models (DDC and SDM),
resembles closely traditional design process stages. It could imply that it is one of the
“characteristics” that systemic design inherited from the broader design field.
Examining existing systemic design process models reveals a sometimes narrow per­
spective on integrating systems thinking. The consideration often focuses on the size
of the system, leading to a limited exploration of other dimensions of complexity, such
as system behaviour and its dynamic nature. Consequently, integration of systemic
thinking is often limited to the practical mapping of the system.
As an emergent field, systemic design offers a substantial amount of literature present­
ing varied perspectives that are often contradictory. The challenge lies in the lack of
alignment regarding the definition of systemic design. This divergence in viewpoints
makes it challenging to develop or evaluate methodologies, as identifying the correct
approaches becomes difficult due to the absence of clear consensus on what consti­
tutes right or wrong in systemic design.
70
==PAGE:76==
5.0.2
Limitations of the study
The systematic literature review was conducted by a single researcher, involving the
reading of abstracts for excluded literature once, and data collection was carried out after
a single reading of the articles. (This does not apply to the main process models, which
underwent thorough analysis.) To enhance the robustness of the study, a reevaluation of
excluded articles and a second round of analysis for included articles could be beneficial,
ensuring that no crucial process models or elements were overlooked.
The initial selection of process models for this study underwent an early review, evalu­
ating them against the boundary critique principle and the level of systems intervention,
specifically focusing on leverage points. To make the assessment more reliable, the de­
cisions made in this study could be reassessed, acknowledging the potential influence
of personal biases and individual interpretations of these two concepts. However, it is
important to note that the study presents these considerations transparently, allowing
for critical analysis. The impact of this assessment on the development of the process
model is substantial, as the most comprehensive models are selected for analysis, guid­
ing the derivation of the process model.
The research’s relevance could have been enhanced by exploring a broader range of
literature, particularly past proceedings of symposiums organized by the Systemic De­
sign Association where researchers in the systemic design field share their most recent
work, and grey literature covering numerous approaches and frameworks proposed by
systemic design practitioners. Additionally, the database search was confined to terms
like ”systemic design” and ”systems­oriented design,” overlooking related terms such
as systems/systemic innovation, systems­led design, design for complexity, or others.
While due to time constraints, the scope of this research was narrowed, expanding the
academic literature review to encompass these branches could enrich the exploration
of the convergence of systems and design sciences.
The recommendations on good practices proposed by systemic design experts did not
receive equal attention. Their relevance was assessed against the defined principles
and the state of the process model at the time. Some concepts were excluded based on
the researcher’s personal experience with frameworks, potentially overlooking propos­
als that could have enhanced the model. Therefore, a more comprehensive examina­
tion of these recommendations could have contributed to a more well­rounded process
model.
5.0.3
Limitations of the process model
The comparison of existing systemic design models in this research has likely been in­
fluenced by the researcher’s personal background in industrial and service design. This
71
==PAGE:77==
background directed the focus towards identifying patterns in the process models rele­
vant to design practice. It is essential to recognize that someone with a different back­
ground might have emphasized or compared different elements in the process models,
potentially enhancing the analysis and steering the derivation of the final process model
in a different direction.
A secondary objective of this research was to explore the feasibility of making the pro­
cess implementable by motivated groups independently, without the direct involvement
of design or research teams (while keeping in mind to maintain the quality of the process
model to be derived). This choice was driven by the perception that systemic design,
with its aim of solving complex societal challenges, cannot always rely on external par­
ties such as designers or research teams. This is especially relevant given the extended
time frames of projects impacted by interventions that may not yield immediate results
in complex systems. Therefore, the question arose: What if the process model en­
couraged motivated groups to take action independently? However, it is essential to
recognize that the expectations placed on these groups are demanding, as the success
of the process relies on participant engagement, their ability to reflect, and reaching
a level of consideration that addresses individual mental models and shared cognitive
frameworks.
This approach was practically tested in an AgriFoodTure workshop, intentionally offer­
ing relatively low levels of facilitation. Despite reaching discussions involving mental
models, there were instances where participants, even when facilitated, avoided less
comfortable topics. As a result, choices were made in the process model development,
recommending the involvement of a facilitator. Additionally, in the derived methodol­
ogy, specific questions were proposed to facilitate such discussions, and tools were
modified to foster deep conversations. However, further investigation into concepts that
could help to facilitate these discussions could be conducted. For example, the science
of facilitation, collaboration, group sense­making, and conflict resolution.
Furthermore, the discussion on the role of designers, primarily perceived as key facilita­
tors in systemic design, remains relevant. Questions arise about how to transition from
external facilitation to empowering organizations to independently undertake these roles
as projects extend over longer time frames. Alternatively, maybe the roles of designers
need to be rethought to bear more responsibility and commitment, enough to facilitate
such projects over extended durations.
However, in contrast to the main process models, the derived process model grants
significantly more agency to the involved actors and omits the role of the designer as a
researcher. Theoretically, the choice aligns with the collaborative mindset of systemic
design and the aspiration for fairness in co­creating a shared future. However, deter­
mining the feasibility of executing such projects continuously in a collaborative setting
requires empirical validation, which might expose a weakness in the derived process
model.
72
==PAGE:78==
The emphasis on relationship building within the system is evident among the principles
of systemic design process models found in academic literature. The derived process
model asserts that having a common purpose or goal in the system creates coalitions
of inherently motivated actors working toward the same goal. However, further analy­
sis into domains such as social network analysis, collaborative behaviour analysis, or
collaborative tools could enhance the investigation of this claim.
The model is constructed around encouraging constant reflective practice that chal­
lenges actors’ understanding of their choices, aiming to prevent potentially destructive
interventions in the system. However, assessing the effects in the system is challeng­
ing, if not impossible, and it is difficult to determine if shifts in mental models are always
positive. Hence, the model cannot guarantee prevention from choices that could cause
rebound effects within the system. Nevertheless, systemic design, along with this pro­
cess model, encourages monitoring and reflection, which might provide the opportunity
to identify negative ”design choices.”
The derived process model varies in the level of prescription among its stages, poten­
tially influenced by the fact that some stages are more ”designerly”, where pragmatic
methods familiar to the researcher are available. The final stage in the model is the
weakest in terms of prescription, as no concrete method exists to assure proper moni­
toring, evaluation, and learning from interventions introduced in the system (as already
discussed in the paragraph above). Scientific and grey literature present this as more
of a mental shift required for systemic designers to cope with the unknown and unex­
pected behaviour of the system and to develop a learning culture within the organization
or community. While this presents a limitation in the process model, there is an opportu­
nity for further research in the systemic design field to make this activity more practical
or tangible.
Finally, this process model can be considered generic, as discussed earlier in this re­
port, elements such as actors and power dynamics are present in every system, making
the process model valuable in a variety of cases. However, it is crucial to question if
such a level of intervention in the system is necessary due to the model’s principle of
transformation and the pursuit of a radical shift in the system. Additionally, the process
model relies on involving participants relevant to the specific problem situation; there­
fore, if there is no opportunity to involve such relevant actors to support the process,
other methods might be more suitable for that specific case.
Limitations of the Equity Approach
The process model is built on the idea of fostering equity, continuously advocating for
reflection on system boundaries and the inclusion of affected actors in the system. The
intention is to enrich the worldviews of participants and provide a choice to make the
73
==PAGE:79==
process more inclusive and diverse. However, it cannot guarantee that actors will make
the right choices. Furthermore, this perspective remains predominantly anthropocentric.
As highlighted by one expert, equity, in her view, involves exploring the relationships
between humans and between humans and the environment. To deepen our under­
standing, we must acknowledge our vulnerability when considering our relationship with
nature [108]. Delving into this interdependence could transform our perception of our
role as actors within the system, fostering truly transformative and equitable changes
not only towards humans but also towards other sentient beings and the environment.
Regarding equity, the process model could be criticized for being solely built on scientific
knowledge, which is often perceived as a phenomenon predominantly rooted in Western
culture. It has been criticized for its ethnocentrism, epistemic injustice, or lack of diver­
sity. To genuinely adhere to the principle of equity, this concept should be expanded
with knowledge from more diverse perspectives, including different fields of science,
practices outside the scientific discourse, or (scientific) literature from various cultures.
5.0.4
Design and Systemic Design
The most substantial difference between conventional design and systemic design lies
in their approach to the concept of a ”solution.” In traditional design processes, research,
development, and evaluation revolve around creating an optimal design aligned with es­
tablished requirements. Conversely, in systemic design, the focus shifts to comprehend­
ing the entire system and its intricate relationships, aiming to identify an optimal point for
intervention. Systemic design often advocates for small interventions or experiments to
facilitate learning about the system, leading to a deeper understanding. However, while
SD process models emphasize the importance of intervention, they often lack detailed
insights into the creation of these interventions. The literature on systemic design ac­
knowledges that defining an optimal intervention is likely impossible. Drawing from the
work of system thinkers such as Meadows D. or Midgley G., the concept of systemic
intervention emerges, encouraging actors to reflect on boundaries, thereby expanding
their mental models and inducing significant changes in the system. However, a cru­
cial question arises: can sparking new understanding and mindset shifts be achieved
more effectively through pragmatic, creative, participatory, and inherently ”designerly”
methods? Furthermore, how can we empower actors to act upon these changes in
mindsets? Is design a tool that can be leveraged to create avenues for spreading new
narratives or novel ways of acting?
In this context, I’d like to add a personal consideration that a process model may not be
the optimal intervention in the systemic design field. It can be viewed as a standardized
process built on predefined outcomes, potentially providing a false sense of comfort.
This type of mindset is desirable in Western culture, however, it may not necessarily
align with the complexity that one has to deal with when designing for complex systems
74
==PAGE:80==
or addressing wicked challenges. I believe that the design’s contribution to the systemic
design field could shift towards building skills and methods within communities, fostering
collective capabilities to navigate complexity, and the unknown, and engage in experi­
mentation, sense­making, adaptation, and the development of a learning culture. This
shift would likely better equip communities to create positive changes within the system.
5.0.5
Recommendations for the AgriFoodTure roadmap revi­
sion process
It’s essential to acknowledge that the roadmap was developed in the spring of 2021,
therefore, requesting interviewees to recollect the process at this point might not have
yielded the most accurate information. Additionally, stakeholders did not provide any
supplementary materials, such as meeting minutes or relevant presentations, to support
this research. Despite these limitations, below are several recommendations aimed at
refining the future revision of the roadmap.
To begin with, AgriFoodTure should adopt a significantly more inclusive approach, in­
volving a diverse group of actors in the process to counterbalance the previous top­
down approach. Mapping and exploring the broader system with a diverse stakeholder
group would bring to light additional themes that should be integrated into the roadmap,
going beyond the existing four tracks. Furthermore, a thorough analysis of the relation­
ship between the tracks and new themes is crucial. Currently, tracks display a tunnel
vision towards specific fields, existing independently rather than interconnectedly. Con­
sequently, activities in one track are likely to affect the other, and those effects are likely
to be overlooked.
In the context of the agri­food system, evident power dynamics exist within sectors and
within actors themselves. Ideally, these relationships should be brought to the surface
and addressed through a dialogical process and in­depth discussions involving con­
flicting parties and all relevant stakeholders. Decisions should not solely benefit the
powerful but should be chosen based on what is considered best for creating space to
deconstruct existing structures within the system, leading to overall openness for sys­
temic change.
While compliments were given for the roadmap’s ”guiding” nature, the goals could be en­
hanced. Instead of focusing solely on objectives that target the lowest level of leverage
points in the system, such as a decrease in CO2 emissions (involving changes in param­
eters or numbers), the proposed goals should also guide interventions that genuinely
transform society’s worldviews and perceptions. For instance, goals could encompass
shifts in attitudes toward diet, food production and consumption, and animal rights.
75
==PAGE:81==
Conclusions
This paper contributes to the research in the systemic design field by presenting an
overview of existing systemic design process models in scientific literature. It provides
a comprehensive comparative assessment, incorporating principles from systemic de­
sign and concepts of system science. The culmination of this effort is the development
of a new systemic design process model, effectively synthesizing existing academic
research into a cohesive body of knowledge.
The development of the process model drew insights from the analysis of systemic de­
sign process models present in scientific literature, incorporating practices and advice
from systemic design experts, and integrating elements from both design and systems
science. The primary objective of this new systemic design process model is to facilitate
an equitable transformation within complex systems.
Built upon the theory of leverage points, the process model proposes that the most
significant system changes occur when the paradigm within the system undergoes a
shift. Consequently, the model involves an examination of the prevailing worldviews,
perceptions, and norms that uphold the current system, along with identifying the sys­
temic powers that sustain this status quo. Actors within the system are prompted to un­
cover elements requiring change. The process actively engages participants in crafting
a new purpose for the future and envisioning a transitional journey towards this reimag­
ined system, intended to disrupt existing cognitive structures. Throughout this iterative
journey, actors’ worldviews are systematically challenged through interventions related
to boundaries and interventions within the system, fostering observation and learning
which deepen the understanding of the system and challenge existing perceptions.
Envisioned as an ongoing cycle, this process expects continuous changes in actors’
mindsets, along with adaptive learning, guiding the system slowly toward the desired
ideal state. In conclusion, this systemic design process model aims to make a mean­
ingful contribution to the ongoing discussion on systemic design approaches in complex
systems.
While the model is grounded in existing scientific knowledge, its validation has been
76
==PAGE:82==
limited to the presentation of the process model and the partial trial of two out of the five
process model stages with a team of experts dealing with a complex challenge. Although
the test yielded the expected outcomes, revealing positive feedback and generating
interest in the systemic design approach, this level of testing falls short of substantiating
the claim that the model is effective in practice. Consequently, further empirical testing
of the process model is essential.
Practitioners intending to implement the process model in real­world scenarios should
be aware that the success of this process model depends on active participation and
participants engaging in profound conversations and reflections. Therefore, robust fa­
cilitation can play a pivotal role in supporting the process and guiding actors in the in­
tended direction. However, it is crucial to acknowledge that, for the same reason, this
process may not be universally applicable, especially in cultures or more hierarchical
settings where individuals may be less inclined to open up, express their feelings, or
voice opinions in a communal setting.
