PM14. Peter H. J. (2014)
Systemic Design Principles
In practice, social systems are not “designed” with a 
prescribed set of design methods or a toolkit. Since the 
social system is a social entity or service ultimately 
defined by its stakeholders, the methods and strategies 
adopted for systemic design must be accepted and 
understood by these stakeholders.
Evaluation of not 
only the system 
but the method 
of evaluation in 
itself
PM48. Norman D. C. (2021)
Design-​driven approach to evaluation (DDE)
role of evaluation in support of systems change is in the demarcation and establishment of the boundaries and 
identification of the perspectives (voices) of those involved in a system, particularly with attention to equity
DE recognises that purposes may 
change as contexts shift, new learning 
is gained, and that this learning can be 
used to support adaptive change in the 
system over time
PM40. Hussaini W. S., Vinnakota T. (2014)
SD application for program management
detailed stakeholder needs and 
expectations/ requirements 
with respect to the program are 
to be defined
 Benefits realization should be the iterative step at each stage of the methodology.
PM.39 Walker-​Munro B (2020)
Cyber- systemics
 leverage on the development and maintenance of a particular relationship or class of relationships, with monitoring permitting the regulator to understand how influence is 
created and transferred within the web of relationships
PM57. Jacoby JP A, Van Ael K. (2021)
SD Process (university course)
Guiding questions on who owns relevant 
knowledge and power to impact the 
local system. To understand the dynamics 
of the systems-​as-​is.
1. Understanding the system
model describes which interventions to 
perform starting from an overall value 
proposition one wishes to obtain on the 
long term. The process is driven by the explicit 
description of a desirable future.
 2. Developing the
 intervention model
Focus on 
added value
Intervention 
models
Enriching 
the 
intervention 
models
Design interventions for specific 
leverage points and to combine

Design-Driven Conflic Relevant Theory	
Stages as described in DDC Methodology
Systemic Design Methodology	
Holistic diagnosis
Holistic diagnosis: the first phase is based on a combination of desk and field research, which aims to investigate the given scenario from an economic, social and 
environmental aspect, considering the flow of energy and matter
PM6 in detail describes the HD process: 
1. Assessment of the Data Framework. Defining boundaries of the system.
2. Research. Desk and field research by designer
3. Collect. Organise data into the format established in Step 1. Allow actors to integrate feedback. Confrontation of ideas between all the actors of the 
multidisciplinary research team.
4. Visualize. Make information understandable to everyone to achieve productive and collaborative horizontal dialogue.
5. Interpretation of Complexity. A qualitative inductive method. Allow everyone to leave feedback. The process should not be overtaken by a designer. visualization 
allows all members of the multidisciplinary research team to have access to the same kind of information to elaborate further analysis of a complex scenario, 
integrating all feedback.
Application of (SDM) in RETRACE project
Holistic diagnosis
1. Regional framework: based on quantitative and qualitative data the visual mapping (infographics, schemas, gigamaps) were made by the systemic designers. 
2. Analysis of current policies: overview of policy state-of-the-art to underline the potential policy gaps that could inhibit transition to CE. Also, understanding of 
regional goals. 
3. Analysis of the principal economic and industrial sectors: analysed policy instruments and the context information, deliver potential synergies at a systemic level 
between different value chains or processes at a regional and interregional scale. This action enabled the peer reviews discussions around the regional data 
presented and generating a knowledge transfer to the other partner countries.

Regional Action Plans (RAP)
The RAP =  strategies described the regional priorities addressing a range of CE policy gaps. In addition, it 
includes planned measures and support actions for the implementation period (2018–2020) and beyond. 
These future-oriented vision actions are pointless if not followed by a real implementation.
The holistic diagnosis assessment of the regional context on regards the CE was essential to elaborate a RAP 
for each region. After matching the results of the holistic diagnosis and the experiences brought from the 
good practices, lead to the identification of six policy gaps common to the partner regions. These explore 
the fields of intervention that could be tackled to support a CE and focus on the several aspects that imply a 
transition to a CE in such regions. 
RAPs made visible the benefits of adopting systemic approaches in 
the transition towards a CE and providing a pathway with necessary 
steps needed to undertake with that purpose, including policy 
recommendations for the update of regional/national Smart 
Specialization strategies to the transition to more systemic 
approaches.
Another component to ensure a successful learning process is the 
involvement of all stakeholders as end-users of the RAP and at the 
same time consider them as key players on disseminating this 
knowledge about CE and SD among their networks.
4. Translation (Problematization, intersegment, enrolment, mobilization ) 
Output
Through these processes (making a controversy context interactive, and create a problem structuring 
approach) the contradiction between people can turn into a competitive form of engagement wherein people 
can not only learn from each other, but they can also utilize their thoughts and opinions through the exchange 
of their interests.'
'Our premise is that this should ideally lead to construction since, on one hand, it can provide a situation of 
social learning that gradually changes the predominant mindset and paradigm within the system (through 
a different moment of translation) and on the other hand, it can lead to the strength of the network being 
maintained with a long-term perspective.' 
Translation aims to bring actors together with conflicts of views or interest to facilitate the ideal of a (new) 
network of allies construction. Through multiple moments of translation the intention is to change the 
learning paradigm of the context. The literal meaning of translation refers to the ability to displace one’s 
opinion and thoughts from a prior context to an ideal situation [30].
For DDC, the translation process is the core of the methodology and is similar to the intervention strategies 
in conventional design methods.
Translation is an opportunistic approach, hence never a completed accomplishment, i.e., it may fail under 
certain conditions. In other words, similar to the outcomes of a design process, the result of a translation is 
not an ultimate solution; it rather creates a condition or a situation for learning and adaptation in response 
to a problematic situation [57]. Therefore, to translate, one must facilitate continuous learning and 
adaptation between different social worlds.
The objective of problematization is to facilitate the creation of new possibility spaces known as passage 
points, essential for exploring new realities. Through problematization, by providing new alternatives  
designer can change actors’ objectives and interests as well as the way they approach the problems.
To facilitate creating new possibilities, for example, a new system of affairs, designers need to create a 
convergence between actors, ideally based on their commonalities.
For the DDC methodology, the negotiation of interests (intersegments) along with the next three steps, 
enrolment and mobilization represent the prototyping phase in a design process
(I) the new passage points i.e. new core stories
(j) the network construction i.e. nominations & negotiations

Action Plan implementation
Output
In the context of policy-making, it is permissible to conceive the implementation impacts to 
go beyond the end of the project (long time-frames). 
This process exposes the “actionable and proactive” mindset of the SD that combines with 
a foresight vision, can achieve concrete outcomes in the short term while addressing 
broader actions in the long term. From this point of view, SD ensures the introduction of 
policies were all participant actors can look after the development of the several actions on 
CE within a short, medium and long term, where the short and medium ones designed to 
support future implementations.
The case of RETRACE delivers an example of how short-term actions on wicked scenarios 
such as the linear economy framed in a holistic approach can deliver a more powerful 
impact on the long-term. This SD approaches on policy proofs that the transition to CE will 
require this kind of methodology as it considers the wicked problems that a transition to the 
CE can bring to European regions
there is an awareness that they cannot modify the current policy status. In this 
perspective, the nature of these short-term actions was from funding opportunities 
for sectors with potential in CE, training workshops on CE, or open reviews of the 
regional strategies. These actions aimed to encourage managing authorities into 
scale up these results into significant impacts on a medium- and long-term policy 
perspective. Therefore, this approach across partners regions, including the 
involvement of all regional actors as end-users can anticipate future strategies on 
the mid—long term horizon, addressing them with today’s changing drivers (e.g., 
research and innovation initiatives, technological development, active economic 
sectors with leading industries, key change-makers).

A.0.1
Workshop Analysis
Please scroll one page down.
100
==PAGE:106==
Workshop  
  
Description and data collection 
The workshop involved six participants: three AgriFoodTure representatives, one scientist from the 
University of Copenhagen, and two representatives from Innovation Fund Denmark. 
The workshop was divided into two parts. In the first, participants were asked to map actors and their 
stakeholders, corresponding to step 1 of the model at that time. They were guided by specific questions: 
mapping stakeholders involved in the roadmap development, mapping stakeholders not involved in the 
mapping development but contributing to or hindering the implementation of the roadmap, and 
mapping stakeholders that would bear the consequences of a successful or unsuccessful 
implementation of the roadmap. 
The second exercise focused on mapping stakeholders according to different types of power. This 
involved identifying those supporting or impeding the change in the system and recognizing actors with 
power over others. Additionally, questions were posed regarding the origins of such power. To 
conclude, participants prioritized the actors they wished to invite in the process of revising the roadmap. 
  
The primary goals were to assess participants' ability to reflect on the importance of inviting a more 
diverse group of stakeholders than in the initial map development and to determine if these exercises 
could stimulate deeper discussions or conflicts within the group, allowing the discussion to reach the 
value levels of the system. 
  
In the second part of the workshop, the systemic design process model was presented. Participants 
received a canvas depicting different stages of the process model and were prompted to reflect on their 
relevance to their specific case. At the conclusion of the workshop, surveys were distributed, containing 
the following questions: 
  
1. Is systemic design applicable in your case? 
2. To what extent this workshop contributes to the planning of the roadmap revision? 
3. Your key takeaways? 
4. What would you have done differently if you were conducting the workshop? 
 
The overarching goal was to evaluate the applicability of the systemic design process model to the 
AgriFoodTure case, specifically in the context of revising the AgriFoodTure roadmap. 
 
Data collection occurred through continuous notetaking during the entire workshop and via a survey 
distributed at the workshop's conclusion. The notes were analyzed with a focus on identifying phrases 
that indicated a deeper level of reflection or reflections on the existing process model. 
 
 
  
101
==PAGE:107==
Evaluation: 
 
Evaluation of the first part of the workshop:  
P.1. Stakeholder mapping:  
The tensions were felt in the room as participants debated the individuals involved or believed to be 
involved in the initial development of the roadmap.  There was disagreement on whether to trust the 
written document or the participants' experiences. 
Expressions of frustration like “Can I call this bullsh*t?” and “I wasn't there, so I don't know” 
underscored the intensity of the discussion. 
Despite a prompt in the middle of the process, participants overlooked mapping the resources of actors. 
Deep reflections on actor mapping unfolded. Participants wondered if actors had begun working against 
each other, considering how this could be perceived as greenwashing by environmental organizations, 
given companies' roles as partners or board members in the process. When asked to map suffering 
actors, one participant deemed it too complex, labeling it a "wicked problem." 
The conversation shifted to an analysis of the system's values, exploring society's dependence on 
comfort and luxury, and the fear. All of them hindering change. The led to discussions about the 
potential end of humanity due to the climate crisis. One participant straightforwardly told another, "You 
really are showing your true face here." 
  
P.1. Power dynamic mapping:  
 
Participants engaged in a productive discussion on power relations within the system. For instance, they 
discussed companies consistently shift between powers to stay afloat in business, responding to 
pressure from consumers. Additionally, there was an acknowledgment that transformative power, 
especially within the government, could be considered failing.  
However, as the discussion delved into total control mechanisms like media, governments, and large 
conglomerates dictating the rest of the system, the atmosphere in the room turned gloomy towards the 
end. 
Ultimately, when asked about the origin of this power, all participants agreed that it stems from the 
system's dependence on money. 
  
P.1. Prioritization 
By the end of the first part of the workshop, participants reached an agreement that only the heads of 
the four main universities were involved in developing the roadmap, with professors from the 
department assisting in the writing. In contrast, the list of participants expanded by at least ten other 
actors that AgriFoodTure plans to involve in the upcoming process. This includes private foundations, 
102
==PAGE:108==
consumers, retail, green NGOs, C20, schools, pupils, and others. The exercises were deemed successful 
as participants decided to broaden the boundaries of the system they would be working with. 
Furthermore, during the final discussion at the end of the workshop, participants mentioned the need to 
involve even more stakeholders. To achieve this, they suggested using a Quadruple Helix model. This 
implies that the tools used at the beginning of the workshop might not have been/felt sufficient. 
 
P.2. Presentation of the process model and reflection on its applicability in the agri-food sector 
 
Reflection on the System model:

Participants responses to the questions: 
  
5. Is systemic design applicable in your case? 
 
Two participants said, "definitely yes," while two said "yes," and one said “maybe”.  
The director of AgriFoodTure stated that it is highly relevant both in relation to updating the roadmap 
and in considering how they can work to achieve their objectives. 
Other points highlighted included the applicability of the bottom-up approach, the significance of 
continuous development, rethinking, evaluation, and working with a mission-oriented focus.  
One participant recommended integrating the concept of wicked problems into the roadmap.  
Additionally, another participant pointed out that some tools might are being implemented already 
without them realizing they have a specific name. 
 
6. To what extent this workshop contributed to the planning of the roadmap revision? 
  
Touched upon areas that they had not considered before and confirmed the expectation regarding the 
amount of work needed to update the roadmap. 
  
It sparked very productive discussions and provided a solid framework to build on. In particular, the part 
about envisioning the future is crucial. 
  
The workshop sparked excellent discussions, input, and a new way of thinking, offering varied 
perspectives and understanding. 
  
It was a valuable inspiration and made it clear how much preparation is necessary to engage the right 
stakeholders and gain meaningful insights. 
  
It was an eye-opener, providing great inspiration to approach complex problems.

approach, and widen the power base. I see many of the tools you brought as "lenses" that can be used 
to examine specific aspects of the mission, the roadmap, the timelines, potential opposing views, etc. 
  
 
7. Your key takeaways? 
  
There is no simple solution. It is a process where you work on different steps and go back and forth 
while learning along the way and implementing your learnings.

Reflection on the workshop:  
  
The primary challenge arose when two key participants could only attend the second part of the 
workshop, necessitating a restructuring of the agenda. The central focus was to understand the 
applicability of the process model to the agri-food sector. Consequently, the last 1 hour and 45 minutes, 
when everyone was present, were dedicated to this crucial aspect. Due to time constraints, the process 
model was presented in a format that didn't allow all participants to practically engage with every stage. 
The other four participants had briefly covered the first two stages earlier in the workshop. As a result, 
their understanding heavily depended on the presentation's quality and the presenter's ability to convey 
complex concepts in a short time. This may explain why certain aspects of the methodology, where the 
description might not have been entirely clear (e.g., coalition building), received less attention during 
the reflection phase. 
 
Secondly, a delicate balance had to be maintained between reflecting on the process model and 
allowing participants to update each other on the project process, as the actors hadn't been closely 
collaborating in the past period. Dynamics of power were present, with AgriFoodTure (three 
participants) considered to own the project process. However, Innovation Fund Denmark (two 
participants, arriving late), was the entity initially initiating the call for agri-food sector roadmaps and 
currently funding the AgriFoodTure organization. For that reason, time had to be devoted to 
accommodating such discussions. 
 
Finally, having only one facilitator in the room responsible for both facilitating the session and gathering 
data posed a challenge. Additionally, there was an attempt to assess whether the participants, left to 
their own devices, could delve into deeper levels of discussion. This dual role and goal may have 
compromised the workshop's facilitation. As one participant pointed out, it seemed that the discussion 
became the goal in itself, whereas a more tangible outcome could have been achieved if participants 
were given more concrete direction. 
107
